وللشفيع الشفعة، ولو كان الشفيع مرتداً فمات أو قتل على الردة أو لحق بدار الحرب فلا شفعة لوارثه ولو كان المرتد لم يلحق بدار الحرب ثم بيعت الدار كان لوارثه الشفعة. وإن اشترى المستأمن داراً ولحق بدار الحرب فالشفيع على شفعته حتى يلقاه، وإن كان الشفيع هو الحربي ودخل دار الحرب بطلت شفعته، وإن كان الشفيع مسلماً أو ذمياً فدخل دار الحرب إن لم يعلم بالبيع فهو على شفعته، وإن علم ودخل ولم يطلب بطلت شفعته. وإن اشترى المسلم داراً في دار الحرب وشفيعها مسلم ثم أسلم أهل الدار فلا شفعة للشفيع وههنا أصل تنبني عليه هذه المسائل يجب العلم به وهو أن كل حكم لا يفتقر إلى قضاء القاضي فدار الإسلام ودار الحرب في ذلك الحكم على حد سواء، وكل حكم مفتقر إلى قضاء القاضي لا يثبت ذلك في حق من كان من المسلمين في دار الحرب بمباشرة ذلك الحكم في دار الحرب ؛ نظير الأول البيع والشراء وصحة الاستيلاء ونقوذ العتق ووجوب الصوم والصلاة فإن هذه الأحكام كلها من أحكام المسلمين وتجري على من كان في دار الحرب من المسلمين. ونظير الثاني الزنا فإن المسلم إذا زنى في دار الحرب ثم دخل دار الإسلام يقام عليه الحد .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And by the equivalent of wine and the value of the pig if the pre-emptor is a dhimmi, and by their value if he is a Muslim) meaning if a dhimmi buys from a dhimmi real estate with wine or a pig, then if its pre-emptor is a dhimmi, he takes it by the equivalent of wine and the value of the pig because this sale with this price is valid among them. When it is valid, the rulings of sale are attached to it, and among the rulings is the obligation of pre-emption, so he deserves it whether dhimmi or Muslim. However, the dhimmi is not prevented from receiving wine, so he takes it by it because it is among fungibles. The Muslim is not able to do that because he is prohibited from owning it and taking possession of it, so its value is obligatory upon him, as we mentioned in the guarantee of aggression. The pig is among non-fungibles, so its value is obligatory upon both. And it is not said that the value of the pig stands in place of its substance because it is qimi, so it must be prohibited for a Muslim to own it, unlike the value of wine as is known in its place. For we say: It is prohibited for him only if the value is a substitute for the pig, but if it is a substitute for something else, it is not prohibited. Here it is a substitute for the house, not for the pig. Rather, the pig is assessed by the value as a substitute for the house, so it is not prohibited for him to own it. If the buyer becomes Muslim before taking the pre-emption, then the pre-emptor takes it by the value of the pig. If the pre-emptor is a Muslim and a dhimmi, each takes half by the aforementioned value of the pig, considering the part as the whole. If the dhimmi becomes Muslim, his ruling becomes that of a Muslim from the beginning, so he takes it by the value of the pig. And if the price was fungible and it was lost before taking the pre-emption, he takes it by its value due to inability. Similarly, the musta'min is like the dhimmi in all the rulings we mentioned because he commits to our laws during his stay in our land. There is no difference whether the buyer is a house or a church or a synagogue, because the pre-emptor takes it by pre-emption because the dhimmi's ownership is established if he believes that his ownership does not cease by making it a church or synagogue. If he believes it ceases, similarly, because by proceeding with the sale he becomes a believer in permissibility. The dhimmi, if he adheres to our religion, his transaction is carried out according to our religion even if it is not permissible, and for this reason, if they litigate before us, we judge by our religion. The apostate has no pre-emption. The method of knowing the value of wine and pig has been mentioned repeatedly. If one of the two contractors becomes Muslim and the wine is not yet taken possession of, the sale is annulled because Islam prevents its possession, but the pre-emption is not invalidated because it was established by the sale, so it is not invalidated by its annulment, just as if he bought a house with a slave and the slave perished before possession, the sale is annulled by its perishing but the pre-emption is not invalidated, so the pre-emptor takes it by the value of the slave. It is restricted that the price is wine or a pig, because if it was carrion or blood, there is no pre-emption for him, as in al-Asl: A Christian buys from a Christian a house with carrion or blood, then there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor. End. The author did not address what if it becomes vinegar then the seller or buyer becomes Muslim, then half of the house is claimed, and the pre-emptor comes and takes half by half of the wine and does not take by half of the vinegar; then the buyer claims from the seller half of the vinegar if it exists, otherwise he claims half of its value. In al-Mabsut: If an apostate sells a house and dies or is killed in apostasy or joins the abode of war, the sale is void and there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor. In al-Sighnaqi: If the seller becomes Muslim before joining the abode of war, the sale is valid and the pre-emptor has pre-emption. If the pre-emptor is an apostate and dies or is killed in apostasy or joins the abode of war, there is no pre-emption for his heir. If the apostate did not join the abode of war and then the house is sold, his heir has pre-emption. If a musta'min buys a house and joins the abode of war, the pre-emptor retains his pre-emption until he meets him. If the pre-emptor is a harbi and enters the abode of war, his pre-emption is invalid. If the pre-emptor is a Muslim or dhimmi and enters the abode of war, if he did not know of the sale, he retains his pre-emption; if he knew and entered without demanding, his pre-emption is invalid. If a Muslim buys a house in the abode of war and its pre-emptor is a Muslim, and then the inhabitants of the house become Muslim, there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor. Here is a principle upon which these issues are built: Every ruling that does not require the judgment of a judge, the abode of Islam and the abode of war are equal in that ruling. Every ruling that requires the judgment of a judge is not established for Muslims in the abode of war by directly performing that ruling there. Example of the first: sale, purchase, validity of seizure, effect of emancipation, obligation of fasting and prayer—all these are rulings of Muslims and apply to Muslims in the abode of war. Example of the second: adultery, because if a Muslim commits adultery in the abode of war and then enters the abode of Islam, the hadd punishment is established against him.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This is a lengthy ruling-with-proof passage expanding Kanz al-Daqaiq's matn about pre-emption when the price is wine or pig. The basic ruling: if the buyer and seller are dhimmis, the sale is valid, so the shafi' (even if Muslim) has the right of pre-emption. For a dhimmi shafi', he gives the equivalent of wine (since it's fungible among them) and the value of the pig (since it's non-fungible). For a Muslim shafi', he gives the value of both because he cannot possess wine or pig. The commentator addresses objections about the prohibition of valuing pig: the value here is a substitute for the house, not the pig, so it is permissible. Then discusses sub-cases: buyer becomes Muslim, shafi' is both Muslim and dhimmi, dhimmi converts, loss of item, musta'min, apostates, and finally a principle about judgments in dar al-harb vs dar al-Islam. The commentary also cites al-Asl, al-Mabsut, and al-Sighnaqi for additional rulings.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Wine, an intoxicating beverage that is impure and prohibited for Muslims.
خنزير (khinzir): Pig, an unclean animal; its sale is invalid for Muslims but valid among dhimmis.
شفيع (shafi'): The pre-emptor, the person entitled to take the property by pre-emption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A dhimmi sells a house to another dhimmi for a pig. The shafi' is a Muslim. What must the shafi' pay?
A) The pig itself
B) The value of the pig
C) Nothing, pre-emption is invalid
D) The equivalent of the pig

Key Takeaway
A Muslim shafi' takes by value when the price is impure, because the value substitutes the property, not the prohibited item.

Subcases: Conversion, Mixed Status, and Loss of Price
Expansion on subcases of the core ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rulings of shuf'a to scenarios where the buyer converts to Islam, where the shafi' is both Muslim and dhimmi, where the dhimmi shafi' converts, and where the fungible price is lost before claim.

Core Idea
Changes in a party's status or the existence of the price do not invalidate shuf'a; compensation is adjusted accordingly.

The earlier lesson established the basic compensation rule. This lesson addresses dynamic situations: what happens if the buyer becomes Muslim before the shafi' takes? What if the shafi' is part Muslim, part dhimmi? What if the dhimmi shafi' himself converts? And what if the price (if fungible) perishes? Each subcase is carefully reasoned, preserving the right of shuf'a while adjusting the compensation. The passage also extends the ruling to musta'min and properties used as churches or synagogues.

Guided Path
1
Case: Buyer converts to Islam before shuf'a claim
If the buyer (who purchased with wine or pig) becomes Muslim before the shafi' takes, the shafi' still has the right to shuf'a, but must give the value of the price (since the Muslim buyer cannot own wine/pig either).
The shafi''s right is not affected by the buyer's conversion.
2
Case: Mixed shafi' (Muslim and dhimmi)
If the shafi' is both a Muslim and a dhimmi (co-owners), each takes half the property. The Muslim pays half the value of the pig, the dhimmi pays half the equivalent of wine and half value of pig? The text says both take by value of pig, considering part as whole. Actually, for the dhimmi, he would normally give wine equivalent and pig value, but when sharing with Muslim, the compensation is unified: both give value of pig? The passage says: 'each takes half by the value of the pig'. This simplifies.
This is a specific ruling for mixed shafi'—each pays value of pig.
3
Case: Dhimmi shafi' converts to Islam
If the dhimmi shafi' converts to Islam before claiming, his ruling becomes that of a Muslim shafi' from the beginning: he must pay the value of the pig, not the equivalent of wine (since wine is no longer permissible for him).
Conversion changes the applicable compensation.
4
Case: Fungible price lost before claim
If the price was fungible (like wine) and it is lost before the shafi' takes, the shafi' pays its value due to inability to give the equivalent. This is analogous to the slave perishing in a sale.
The right of shuf'a remains despite the price being lost.
5
Case: Musta'min and church/synagogue
A musta'min (non-Muslim with temporary protection) is treated like a dhimmi in these rulings. The shuf'a also applies if the buyer purchases a church or synagogue, as the dhimmi's ownership is recognized.
The dhimmi's belief about ownership does not affect shuf'a.

قال رحمه الله : (وبمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير إن كان الشفيع ذمياً وبقيمتها لو مسلما) يعني إذا اشترى ذمي من ذمي عقاراً بخمر أو خنزير فإن كان شفيعها ذمياً أخذها بمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير لأن هذا البيع بهذا الثمن صحيح فيما بينهم، فإذا صح رتب عليه أحكام البيع ومن جملة الأحكام وجوب الشفعة فيستحقه ذمياً كان أو مسلماً، غير أن الذمي لا يتعذر عليه تسلم الخمر فيأخذها به لأنه من ذوات الأمثال والمسلم لا يقدر على ذلك لكونه ممنوعاً من تمليكه وتملكه فيجب عليه قيمته كما ذكرنا في ضمان العدوان الخنزير من ذوات القيم فيجب عليهما قيمته ولا يقال قيمة الخنزير تقوم مقام عينه لأنه قيمي فوجب أن يحرم على المسلم تمليكه بخلاف قيمة الخمر على ما عرف في موضعه لأنا نقول: إنما يحرم عليه إذا كانت القيمة بدلاً عن الخنزير، وأما إذا كانت بدلاً عن غيره فلا يحرم وههنا بدل عن الدار لا عن الخنزير، وإنما الخنزير مقدر بقيمة بدل الدار فلا يحرم عليه تمليكها، فإن أسلم المشتري قبل الأخذ بالشفعة فإن الشفيع يأخذها بقيمة الخنزير، ولو كان شفيعها مسلماً وذمياً أخذ كل واحد منهما النصف بما ذكرنا من قيمة الخنزير اعتباراً للبعض بالكل. ولو أسلم الذمي صار حكمه حكم المسلم من الابتداء فيأخذها بقيمة الخنزير والخنزير كما إذا كان الثمن مثلياً فانقطع قبل الأخذ بالشفعة فإنه يأخذها بقيمته للتعذر كذا هذا والمستأمن كالذمي في جميع ما ذكرنا من الأحكام لالتزامه أحكامنا مدة مقامه في دارنا ولا فرق بين أن يكون المشتري داراً أو بيعة أو كنيسة فإن الشفيع يأخذها بالشفعة لأن ملك الذمي فيها ثابت إذا كان يعتقد أن ملكه لا يزول بجعله بيعة أو كنيسة وإن كان يعتقد أنه يزول فكذلك أيضاً لأنه بالإقدام على البيع صار معتقداً الجواز والذمي إذا أدان بديننا ينفذ تصرفه على مقتضى ديننا وإن كان لا يجوز ولهذا لو ترافعا إلينا نحكم بديننا والمرتد لا شفعة له. وطريق معرفة قيمة الخمر والخنزير تقدم مراراً. ولو أسلم أحد المتعاقدين والخمر غير مقبوض انتقض البيع لأن الإسلام يمنع قبضها ولكن لا تبطل الشفعة لأنها وجبت بالبيع فلا تبطل بانتقاضه كما إذا اشتری داراً بعبد فهلك العبد قبل القبض فإن البيع ينتقض بهلاكه ولكن لا تبطل الشفعة فيأخذها الشفيع بقيمة العبد. قيد بكون الثمن خمراً أو خنزيراً لأنه لو كان ميتة أو دماً فلا شفعة له لما في الأصل : اشتری نصراني من نصراني داراً بميتة أو دم فلا شفعة للشفيع .اهـ. ولم يتعرض المؤلف لما إذا صار خلاً ثم أسلم البائع أو المشتري ثم استحق نصف الدار وحضر الشفيع فيأخذ النصف بنصف الخمر ولا يأخذ بنصف الخل ثم يرجع المشتري على البائع بنصف الخل إن كان قائماً، رجع عليه بنصف قيمة الخل. وفي المبسوط : باع المرتد داراً فمات أو قتل على الردة أو لحق بدار الحرب بطل البيع ولا شفعة للشفيع. وفي السغناقي : ولو أسلم البائع قبل اللحوق بدار الحرب جاز البيع وللشفيع الشفعة، ولو كان الشفيع مرتداً فمات أو قتل على الردة أو لحق بدار الحرب فلا شفعة لوارثه ولو كان المرتد لم يلحق بدار الحرب ثم بيعت الدار كان لوارثه الشفعة. وإن اشترى المستأمن داراً ولحق بدار الحرب فالشفيع على شفعته حتى يلقاه، وإن كان الشفيع هو الحربي ودخل دار الحرب بطلت شفعته، وإن كان الشفيع مسلماً أو ذمياً فدخل دار الحرب إن لم يعلم بالبيع فهو على شفعته، وإن علم ودخل ولم يطلب بطلت شفعته. وإن اشترى المسلم داراً في دار الحرب وشفيعها مسلم ثم أسلم أهل الدار فلا شفعة للشفيع وههنا أصل تنبني عليه هذه المسائل يجب العلم به وهو أن كل حكم لا يفتقر إلى قضاء القاضي فدار الإسلام ودار الحرب في ذلك الحكم على حد سواء، وكل حكم مفتقر إلى قضاء القاضي لا يثبت ذلك في حق من كان من المسلمين في دار الحرب بمباشرة ذلك الحكم في دار الحرب ؛ نظير الأول البيع والشراء وصحة الاستيلاء ونقوذ العتق ووجوب الصوم والصلاة فإن هذه الأحكام كلها من أحكام المسلمين وتجري على من كان في دار الحرب من المسلمين. ونظير الثاني الزنا فإن المسلم إذا زنى في دار الحرب ثم دخل دار الإسلام يقام عليه الحد .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And by the equivalent of wine and the value of the pig if the pre-emptor is a dhimmi, and by their value if he is a Muslim) meaning if a dhimmi buys from a dhimmi real estate with wine or a pig, then if its pre-emptor is a dhimmi, he takes it by the equivalent of wine and the value of the pig because this sale with this price is valid among them. When it is valid, the rulings of sale are attached to it, and among the rulings is the obligation of pre-emption, so he deserves it whether dhimmi or Muslim. However, the dhimmi is not prevented from receiving wine, so he takes it by it because it is among fungibles. The Muslim is not able to do that because he is prohibited from owning it and taking possession of it, so its value is obligatory upon him, as we mentioned in the guarantee of aggression. The pig is among non-fungibles, so its value is obligatory upon both. And it is not said that the value of the pig stands in place of its substance because it is qimi, so it must be prohibited for a Muslim to own it, unlike the value of wine as is known in its place. For we say: It is prohibited for him only if the value is a substitute for the pig, but if it is a substitute for something else, it is not prohibited. Here it is a substitute for the house, not for the pig. Rather, the pig is assessed by the value as a substitute for the house, so it is not prohibited for him to own it. If the buyer becomes Muslim before taking the pre-emption, then the pre-emptor takes it by the value of the pig. If the pre-emptor is a Muslim and a dhimmi, each takes half by the aforementioned value of the pig, considering the part as the whole. If the dhimmi becomes Muslim, his ruling becomes that of a Muslim from the beginning, so he takes it by the value of the pig. And if the price was fungible and it was lost before taking the pre-emption, he takes it by its value due to inability. Similarly, the musta'min is like the dhimmi in all the rulings we mentioned because he commits to our laws during his stay in our land. There is no difference whether the buyer is a house or a church or a synagogue, because the pre-emptor takes it by pre-emption because the dhimmi's ownership is established if he believes that his ownership does not cease by making it a church or synagogue. If he believes it ceases, similarly, because by proceeding with the sale he becomes a believer in permissibility. The dhimmi, if he adheres to our religion, his transaction is carried out according to our religion even if it is not permissible, and for this reason, if they litigate before us, we judge by our religion. The apostate has no pre-emption. The method of knowing the value of wine and pig has been mentioned repeatedly. If one of the two contractors becomes Muslim and the wine is not yet taken possession of, the sale is annulled because Islam prevents its possession, but the pre-emption is not invalidated because it was established by the sale, so it is not invalidated by its annulment, just as if he bought a house with a slave and the slave perished before possession, the sale is annulled by its perishing but the pre-emption is not invalidated, so the pre-emptor takes it by the value of the slave. It is restricted that the price is wine or a pig, because if it was carrion or blood, there is no pre-emption for him, as in al-Asl: A Christian buys from a Christian a house with carrion or blood, then there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor. End. The author did not address what if it becomes vinegar then the seller or buyer becomes Muslim, then half of the house is claimed, and the pre-emptor comes and takes half by half of the wine and does not take by half of the vinegar; then the buyer claims from the seller half of the vinegar if it exists, otherwise he claims half of its value. In al-Mabsut: If an apostate sells a house and dies or is killed in apostasy or joins the abode of war, the sale is void and there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor. In al-Sighnaqi: If the seller becomes Muslim before joining the abode of war, the sale is valid and the pre-emptor has pre-emption. If the pre-emptor is an apostate and dies or is killed in apostasy or joins the abode of war, there is no pre-emption for his heir. If the apostate did not join the abode of war and then the house is sold, his heir has pre-emption. If a musta'min buys a house and joins the abode of war, the pre-emptor retains his pre-emption until he meets him. If the pre-emptor is a harbi and enters the abode of war, his pre-emption is invalid. If the pre-emptor is a Muslim or dhimmi and enters the abode of war, if he did not know of the sale, he retains his pre-emption; if he knew and entered without demanding, his pre-emption is invalid. If a Muslim buys a house in the abode of war and its pre-emptor is a Muslim, and then the inhabitants of the house become Muslim, there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor. Here is a principle upon which these issues are built: Every ruling that does not require the judgment of a judge, the abode of Islam and the abode of war are equal in that ruling. Every ruling that requires the judgment of a judge is not established for Muslims in the abode of war by directly performing that ruling there. Example of the first: sale, purchase, validity of seizure, effect of emancipation, obligation of fasting and prayer—all these are rulings of Muslims and apply to Muslims in the abode of war. Example of the second: adultery, because if a Muslim commits adultery in the abode of war and then enters the abode of Islam, the hadd punishment is established against him.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This is a lengthy ruling-with-proof passage expanding Kanz al-Daqaiq's matn about pre-emption when the price is wine or pig. The basic ruling: if the buyer and seller are dhimmis, the sale is valid, so the shafi' (even if Muslim) has the right of pre-emption. For a dhimmi shafi', he gives the equivalent of wine (since it's fungible among them) and the value of the pig (since it's non-fungible). For a Muslim shafi', he gives the value of both because he cannot possess wine or pig. The commentator addresses objections about the prohibition of valuing pig: the value here is a substitute for the house, not the pig, so it is permissible. Then discusses sub-cases: buyer becomes Muslim, shafi' is both Muslim and dhimmi, dhimmi converts, loss of item, musta'min, apostates, and finally a principle about judgments in dar al-harb vs dar al-Islam. The commentary also cites al-Asl, al-Mabsut, and al-Sighnaqi for additional rulings.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters dar al-Islam with temporary safe conduct (aman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a dhimmi buys a house from another dhimmi with wine, and the buyer converts to Islam before the Muslim shafi' claims, what must the shafi' pay?
A) The wine itself
B) The value of the wine
C) Nothing, shuf'a is invalid
D) Half the wine and half its value

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a is a robust right that survives changes in the status of parties or the existence of the price; only the method of compensation adjusts.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
12 min
Apostates, Musta'min, and the Dar al-Harb Principle
Continuation of subcases and a usul principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effects of apostasy, musta'min status, and dar al-harb jurisdiction on the shuf'a right, and explain the usul principle that rulings not requiring a judge's intervention apply equally in dar al-harb and dar al-Islam.

Core Idea
Apostasy and harbi status can void shuf'a; but musta'min retains rights like a dhimmi; and some rulings apply regardless of jurisdiction.

This final lesson from the passage covers edge cases and a key principle. It begins by contrasting wine/pig with carrion and blood—which do not give rise to shuf'a. Then it discusses the vinegar case. The focus then shifts to apostates: a sale by an apostate who dies or leaves dar al-Islam is void, and no shuf'a exists. But if the apostate seller becomes Muslim before leaving, the sale is valid and shuf'a applies. The lesson also addresses the musta'min who joins dar al-harb, and the harbi pre-emptor. Finally, it presents a general usul principle: rulings that do not require a qadi's judgment (like sale, purchase, prayer) operate in dar al-harb for Muslims, while those that require the judge (like hadd for zina) do not. This principle helps explain many of the cited cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the threshold: carrion and blood versus wine and pig
The passage notes that if the price is carrion or blood (which are never permissible for any party), no shuf'a exists at all. This is a boundary condition.
Even among dhimmis, carrion and blood are not valid prices.
2
Examine the vinegar case
If the wine turns into vinegar before the shafi' claims, the rules change. The passage cites a scenario: if wine becomes vinegar, then the buyer claims half the house, the shafi' takes half by half the wine (not vinegar) and the buyer gets compensation from the seller. This is a subtle subcase.
The conversion of the price affects the distribution of claims.
3
Analyze the apostate rulings
An apostate's sale is void if he dies or leaves dar al-Islam in apostasy. No shuf'a for the shafi'. But if the apostate returns to Islam before leaving, the sale is valid and shuf'a exists. The shafi''s apostasy also voids his right, and his heirs do not inherit it unless he had not yet joined dar al-harb.
Apostasy fundamentally changes legal capacity regarding property.
4
Study the musta'min and harbi cases
A musta'min who buys a house and then joins dar al-harb does not lose the shafi''s right; the shafi' retains it until they meet. But if the shafi' himself is a harbi and enters dar al-harb, his right is lost. For a Muslim or dhimmi shafi' who enters dar al-harb after knowing of the sale without demanding, his right is lost.
Jurisdiction and knowledge affect the exercise of shuf'a.
5
Grasp the general principle
Rulings not requiring a judge (e.g., sale, fasting) apply in dar al-harb as in dar al-Islam. Rulings requiring a judge (e.g., hadd for zina) do not take effect in dar al-harb but become enforceable upon entering dar al-Islam.
This principle explains why shuf'a (which requires a judge's enforcement) might be affected by jurisdiction.

قال رحمه الله : (وبمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير إن كان الشفيع ذمياً وبقيمتها لو مسلما) يعني إذا اشترى ذمي من ذمي عقاراً بخمر أو خنزير فإن كان شفيعها ذمياً أخذها بمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير لأن هذا البيع بهذا الثمن صحيح فيما بينهم، فإذا صح رتب عليه أحكام البيع ومن جملة الأحكام وجوب الشفعة فيستحقه ذمياً كان أو مسلماً، غير أن الذمي لا يتعذر عليه تسلم الخمر فيأخذها به لأنه من ذوات الأمثال والمسلم لا يقدر على وإن اشترى المسلم داراً في دار الحرب وشفيعها مسلم ثم أسلم أهل الدار فلا شفعة للشفيع وههنا أصل تنبني عليه هذه المسائل يجب العلم به وهو أن كل حكم لا يفتقر إلى قضاء القاضي فدار الإسلام ودار الحرب في ذلك الحكم على حد سواء، وكل حكم مفتقر إلى قضاء القاضي لا يثبت ذلك في حق من كان من المسلمين في دار الحرب بمباشرة ذلك الحكم في دار الحرب ؛ نظير الأول البيع والشراء وصحة الاستيلاء ونقوذ العتق ووجوب الصوم والصلاة فإن هذه الأحكام كلها من أحكام المسلمين وتجري على من كان في دار الحرب من المسلمين. ونظير الثاني الزنا فإن المسلم إذا زنى في دار الحرب ثم دخل دار الإسلام يقام عليه الحد .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And by the equivalent of wine and the value of the pig if the pre-emptor is a dhimmi, and by their value if he is a Muslim) meaning if a dhimmi buys from a dhimmi real estate with wine or a pig, then if its pre-emptor is a dhimmi, he takes it by the equivalent of wine and the value of the pig because this sale with this price is valid among them. When it is valid, the rulings of sale are attached to it, and among the rulings is the obligation of pre-emption, so he deserves it whether dhimmi or Muslim. However, the dhimmi is not prevented from receiving wine, so he takes it by it because it is among fungibles. The Muslim is not able to do that because he is prohibited from owning it and taking possession of it, so its value is obligatory upon him, as we mentioned in the guarantee of aggression. The pig is among non-fungibles, so its value is obligatory upon both. And it is not said that the value of the pig stands in place of its substance because it is qimi, so it must be prohibited for a Muslim to own it, unlike the value of wine as is known in its place. For we say: It is prohibited for him only if the value is a substitute for the pig, but if it is a substitute for something else, it is not prohibited. Here it is a substitute for the house, not for the pig. Rather, the pig is assessed by the value as a substitute for the house, so it is not prohibited for him to own it. If the buyer becomes Muslim before taking the pre-emption, then the pre-emptor takes it by the value of the pig. If the pre-emptor is a Muslim and a dhimmi, each takes half by the aforementioned value of the pig, considering the part as the whole. If the dhimmi becomes Muslim, his ruling becomes that of a Muslim from the beginning, so he takes it by the value of the pig. And if the price was fungible and it was lost before taking the pre-emption, he takes it by its value due to inability. Similarly, the musta'min is like the dhimmi in all the rulings we mentioned because he commits to our laws during his stay in our land. There is no difference whether the buyer is a house or a church or a synagogue, because the pre-emptor takes it by pre-emption because the dhimmi's ownership is established if he believes that his ownership does not cease by making it a church or synagogue. If he believes it ceases, similarly, because by proceeding with the sale he becomes a believer in permissibility. The dhimmi, if he adheres to our religion, his transaction is carried out according to our religion even if it is not permissible, and for this reason, if they litigate before us, we judge by our religion. The apostate has no pre-emption. The method of knowing the value of wine and pig has been mentioned repeatedly. If one of the two contractors becomes Muslim and the wine is not yet taken possession of, the sale is annulled because Islam prevents its possession, but the pre-emption is not invalidated because it was established by the sale, so it is not invalidated by its annulment, just as if he bought a house with a slave and the slave perished before possession, the sale is annulled by its perishing but the pre-emption is not invalidated, so the pre-emptor takes it by the value of the slave. It is restricted that the price is wine or a pig, because if it was carrion or blood, there is no pre-emption for him, as in al-Asl: A Christian buys from a Christian a house with carrion or blood, then there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor. End. The author did not address what if it becomes vinegar then the seller or buyer becomes Muslim, then half of the house is claimed, and the pre-emptor comes and takes half by half of the wine and does not take by half of the vinegar; then the buyer claims from the seller half of the vinegar if it exists, otherwise he claims half of its value. In al-Mabsut: If an apostate sells a house and dies or is killed in apostasy or joins the abode of war, the sale is void and there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor. In al-Sighnaqi: If the seller becomes Muslim before joining the abode of war, the sale is valid and the pre-emptor has pre-emption. If the pre-emptor is an apostate and dies or is killed in apostasy or joins the abode of war, there is no pre-emption for his heir. If the apostate did not join the abode of war and then the house is sold, his heir has pre-emption. If a musta'min buys a house and joins the abode of war, the pre-emptor retains his pre-emption until he meets him. If the pre-emptor is a harbi and enters the abode of war, his pre-emption is invalid. If the pre-emptor is a Muslim or dhimmi and enters the abode of war, if he did not know of the sale, he retains his pre-emption; if he knew and entered without demanding, his pre-emption is invalid. If a Muslim buys a house in the abode of war and its pre-emptor is a Muslim, and then the inhabitants of the house become Muslim, there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor. Here is a principle upon which these issues are built: Every ruling that does not require the judgment of a judge, the abode of Islam and the abode of war are equal in that ruling. Every ruling that requires the judgment of a judge is not established for Muslims in the abode of war by directly performing that ruling there. Example of the first: sale, purchase, validity of seizure, effect of emancipation, obligation of fasting and prayer—all these are rulings of Muslims and apply to Muslims in the abode of war. Example of the second: adultery, because if a Muslim commits adultery in the abode of war and then enters the abode of Islam, the hadd punishment is established against him.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This is a lengthy ruling-with-proof passage expanding Kanz al-Daqaiq's matn about pre-emption when the price is wine or pig. The basic ruling: if the buyer and seller are dhimmis, the sale is valid, so the shafi' (even if Muslim) has the right of pre-emption. For a dhimmi shafi', he gives the equivalent of wine (since it's fungible among them) and the value of the pig (since it's non-fungible). For a Muslim shafi', he gives the value of both because he cannot possess wine or pig. The commentator addresses objections about the prohibition of valuing pig: the value here is a substitute for the house, not the pig, so it is permissible. Then discusses sub-cases: buyer becomes Muslim, shafi' is both Muslim and dhimmi, dhimmi converts, loss of item, musta'min, apostates, and finally a principle about judgments in dar al-harb vs dar al-Islam. The commentary also cites al-Asl, al-Mabsut, and al-Sighnaqi for additional rulings.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): An apostate, a Muslim who has left Islam.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): The abode of war, territories not under Islamic rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a Muslim pre-emptor (shafi') enters dar al-harb knowing about the sale without making a demand, what happens to his right of pre-emption?
A) It is valid forever
B) It is invalid
C) It remains until the buyer also leaves
D) It is inherited by his heirs

Key Takeaway
Apostasy and jurisdiction significantly affect shuf'a; the principle that some rulings require a judge explains these variations.

Shuf'a Involving Dhimmis and Musta'min with Unlawful Consideration
Rulings on shuf'a involving non-Muslims and unlawful consideration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on shuf'a when the price is wine or pork and explain why shuf'a persists even if the sale is rescinded, distinguishing between dhimmis, musta'min, apostates, and harbi.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is tied to the contract, not the price; it survives even if the sale is rescinded.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses unique scenarios in shuf'a (right of preemption) when the property is sold to or by a non-Muslim, or when the consideration is unlawful (wine, pork). The Hanafi school holds that shuf'a is established by the contract itself, and later impossibility of delivering the price does not extinguish the right. We will work through the key rulings, including how dhimmis and musta'min are treated alike, and how apostasy affects shuf'a.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling on shuf'a with an unlawful price
Read the first part: the shafee (preemptor) takes the property at the value of the wine/pork, not the item itself. This is analogous to a mithli price that perishes before delivery.
The key analogy is the case of a fungible (mithli) that becomes unavailable—the shafee pays its value.
2
Compare the rule for dhimmi and musta'min
The text states that a musta'min (non-Muslim with safe passage) is treated like a dhimmi in all these rulings because he submits to Islamic law during his stay.
This equality simplifies the application: all non-Muslims under Islamic authority follow the same shuf'a rules.
3
Analyze the effect of a party embracing Islam after the sale
If one party becomes Muslim before taking possession of wine, the sale is rescinded (since a Muslim cannot possess wine), but shuf'a remains valid. The shafee still takes the property at the value of the wine.
Shuf'a is independent of the price's actual delivery; it attaches to the contract.
4
Distinguish cases involving apostates and harbi
An apostate's sale is invalid if he dies/lapses or joins dar al-harb, so no shuf'a arises. For a musta'min who buys and then returns to dar al-harb, the shafee's right continues until meeting. A harbi shafee loses his right upon entering dar al-harb.
The shafee's own status matters: once he becomes a harbi (enemy combatant), his right is nullified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكمه حكم المسلم من الابتداء فيأخذها بقيمة الخنزير والخنزير كما إذا كان الثمن مثلياً فانقطع قبل الأخذ بالشفعة فإنه يأخذها بقيمته للتعذر كذا هذا والمستأمن كالذمي في جميع ما ذكرنا من الأحكام لالتزامه أحكامنا مدة مقامه في دارنا ولا فرق بين أن يكون المشتري داراً أو بيعة أو كنيسة فإن الشفيع يأخذها بالشفعة لأن ملك الذمي فيها ثابت إذا كان يعتقد أن ملكه لا يزول بجعله بيعة أو كنيسة وإن كان يعتقد أنه يزول فكذلك أيضاً لأنه بالإقدام على البيع صار معتقداً الجواز والذمي إذا أدان بديننا ينفذ تصرفه على مقتضى ديننا وإن كان في يجوز ولهذا لو ترافعا إلينا نحكم بديننا والمرتد لا شفعة له. وطريق معرفة قيمة الخمر والخنزير تقدم مراراً. ولو أسلم أحد المتعاقدين والخمر غير مقبوض انتقض البيع لأن الإسلام يمنع قبضها ولكن لا تبطل الشفعة لأنها وجبت بالبيع فلا تبطل بانتقاضه كما إذا اشتری داراً بعبد فهلك العبد قبل القبض فإن البيع ينتقض بهلاكه ولكن لا تبطل الشفعة فيأخذها الشفيع بقيمة العبد. قيد بكون الثمن خمراً أو خنزيراً لأنه لو كان ميتة أو دماً فلا شفعة له لما في الأصل : اشتری نصراني من نصراني داراً بميتة أو دم فلا شفعة للشفيع .اهـ. ولم يتعرض المؤلف لما إذا صار خلاً ثم أسلم البائع أو المشتري ثم استحق نصف الدار وحضر الشفيع فيأخذ النصف بنصف الخمر ولا يأخذ بنصف الخل ثم يرجع المشتري على البائع بنصف الخل إن كان قائماً، رجع عليه بنصف قيمة الخل. وفي المبسوط : باع المرتد داراً فمات أو قتل على الردة أو لحق بدار الحرب بطل البيع ولا شفعة للشفيع. وفي السغناقي : ولو أسلم البائع قبل اللحوق بدار الحرب جاز البيع وللشفيع الشفعة، ولو كان الشفيع مرتداً فمات أو قتل على الردة أو لحق بدار الحرب فلا شفعة لوارثه ولو كان المرتد لم يلحق بدار الحرب ثم بيعت الدار كان لوارثه الشفعة. وإن اشترى المستأمن داراً ولحق بدار الحرب فالشفيع على شفعته حتى يلقاه، وإن كان الشفيع هو الحربي ودخل دار الحرب بطلت شفعته، وإن كان الشفيع مسلماً أو ذمياً فدخل دار الحرب إن لم يعلم بالبيع فهو على شفعته، وإن علم ودخل ولم يطلب بطلت شفعته. وإن اشترى المسلم داراً في دار الحرب وشفيعها مسلم ثم أسلم أهل الدار فلا شفعة للشفيع
Its ruling is the ruling of a Muslim from the beginning, so he takes it at the value of pork and wine, just as if the price was a fungible (mithli) and it perished before taking by shuf'a, then he takes it at its value due to impossibility. Similarly, the musta'min is like the dhimmi in all the rulings we have mentioned, because of his commitment to our laws during his stay in our land. There is no difference whether the buyer is a house, a church, or a monastery; the shafee takes it by shuf'a because the dhimmi's ownership is established in it if he believes that his ownership is not nullified by making it a church or monastery. And even if he believes that it is nullified, it is also like that, because by proceeding with the sale he becomes a believer in its permissibility. A dhimmi who judges by our law, his transaction is executed according to our law, even if it is not permissible in his religion. For this, if they come to us for judgment, we judge by our law. An apostate has no shuf'a. The method of knowing the value of wine and pork has been mentioned many times. If one of the contracting parties becomes Muslim and the wine is not yet taken possession of, the sale is rescinded because Islam prevents its possession, but shuf'a is not invalidated because it was established by the sale, so it is not invalidated by its rescission, as in the case of someone who bought a house for a slave, and the slave perished before possession; the sale is rescinded by its perishment, but shuf'a is not invalidated, so the shafee takes it at the value of the slave. It is conditioned that the price is wine or pork, because if it were dead meat or blood, then there is no shuf'a for him, as in al-Asl: A Christian bought a house from a Christian with dead meat or blood; there is no shuf'a for the shafee. End. The author did not address the case where it becomes vinegar, then the seller or buyer becomes Muslim, then half the house is recovered, and the shafee appears; he takes half with half of the wine, and does not take with half of the vinegar; then the buyer recovers from the seller half of the vinegar if it still exists; if it is perished, he recovers half of its value. In al-Mabsut: An apostate sold a house, then died or was killed in apostasy, or joined dar al-harb; the sale is invalidated and there is no shuf'a for the shafee. In al-Sighnaqi: If the seller became Muslim before joining dar al-harb, the sale is valid and the shafee has shuf'a. If the shafee was an apostate and died or was killed in apostasy, or joined dar al-harb, then there is no shuf'a for his heir. If the apostate had not yet joined dar al-harb and then the house was sold, his heir has shuf'a. If a musta'min bought a house and then joined dar al-harb, the shafee remains on his shuf'a until he meets him. If the shafee is a harbi and enters dar al-harb, his shuf'a is nullified. If the shafee is a Muslim or a dhimmi and enters dar al-harb, if he did not know of the sale, he remains on his shuf'a; if he knew and entered without demanding, his shuf'a is nullified. If a Muslim bought a house in dar al-harb and his shafee is a Muslim, then the inhabitants of the house become Muslim, there is no shuf'a for the shafee.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings about shuf'a (right of preemption) when the parties are non-Muslims or the consideration is unlawful (wine, pork). The main point is that shuf'a is established by the sale contract, and even if the contract is later invalidated due to inability to take possession of unlawful consideration (e.g., wine), the shuf'a right remains, analogous to the case where a slave perishes before delivery. The text covers: (1) A Muslim buyer from a dhimmi with wine/pork as price takes the property at the value of the wine/pork (since the wine/pork can't be possessed by the Muslim). (2) A musta'min is treated like a dhimmi in all these rulings because he submits to Islamic law while in dar al-Islam. (3) The property purchased can be a church or monastery; the shafee still gets preemption because the dhimmi owner's ownership is valid in their belief, and even if they believe ownership is nullified, they have by the sale acted as if it's permissible. (4) If one party becomes Muslim before taking possession of wine, the sale is rescinded (since a Muslim cannot possess wine), but shuf'a persists. (5) If the price is dead meat or blood, shuf'a is not established because such items are not legally valuable (mal). (6) If the buyer or seller becomes Muslim after the wine turns to vinegar, the shafee takes with the value of the original wine, not the vinegar. (7) An apostate (murtadd) has no shuf'a, and his sale is invalid if he dies in apostasy or joins dar al-harb. (8) For a musta'min who buys and then returns to dar al-harb, the shafee's right remains until they meet. (9) If the shafee himself is a harbi and enters dar al-harb, his right is nullified. (10) A Muslim buying in dar al-harb with a Muslim shafee: if the inhabitants become Muslim, the shafee's right is lost because the property is now in dar al-Islam? (The text is brief, but there's more detail in the following principle). All these rulings are based on the principle that shuf'a is tied to the contract, not to the possession of the consideration.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of a partner (shafee) to preempt the sale of a jointly-owned property by substituting himself in the buyer's place.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of the Islamic state, paying jizya.
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim from dar al-harb who is granted safe passage (aman) to enter dar al-Islam temporarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Muslim buys a house from a dhimmi with wine as the price, and the wine still exists, what does the shafee pay?
A) The same wine
B) The value of the wine at the time of sale
C) Nothing; shuf'a is invalid
D) The price in cash equal to the wine's value

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a is tied to the sale contract; even if the contract is rescinded due to impossibility of delivering the price, the shafee's right persists and he pays the value of the price.

Shafee's Option: Forcing Removal or Paying for Improvements
Shafee's options when buyer built/planted on preempted land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi (zahir al-riwaya) position on the shafee's options when the buyer has built or planted on preempted property, and explain the qiyas with the mortgagor.

Core Idea
The shafee can force removal because the buyer built on land with a pending preemptive right, like a mortgagor on mortgaged property.

Shuf'a grants the shafee a powerful preemptive right, but what if the buyer has already built a house or planted trees? Must the shafee accept the improvements or can he demand removal? This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the Hanafi school's definitive view (zahir al-riwaya), contrasting it with the position of Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Malik. The key legal reasoning hinges on whether the buyer is considered a wrongdoer (mut'addin) and on an analogy to the mortgagor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The buyer has built or planted on land that is later claimed via shuf'a. Can the shafee force the buyer to remove the improvements?
The core question: who bears the cost of the improvements?
2
State the Hanafi position (zahir al-riwaya)
The shafee has two options: take the land with improvements by paying their value when removed (qima mqlu'an), or force the buyer to remove them and take the land empty.
This is attributed to Abu Hanifa and is the mufta bihi view.
3
Identify the opposing view
Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Malik hold that the shafee cannot force removal; he may only take the property by paying the value of improvements or leave it.
Their reasoning: the buyer is not a wrongdoer; forcing removal causes greater harm.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response uses qiyas: the buyer built on property with a firm right of another (shuf'a) without permission, analogous to a mortgagor building on mortgaged property. Such acts are nullified and removal can be compelled.
Distinguish from a donee or buyer in a void purchase, who acted with owner's permission.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, under the Hanafi view, the shafee can clear the land; under the opposing view, he must pay for improvements if he wants the land.
This difference impacts the financial burden on the shafee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وقيمة البناء والغرس لو بنى المشتري أو غرس أو كلف قلعهما) يعني إذا بنى المشتري أو غرس في الأرض المشفوعة ثم قضى للشفيع بالشفعة فالشفيع بالخيار إن شاء أخذها بالثمن وقيمة البناء والغرس مقلوعاً، وإن شاء كلف المشتري قلعه فيأخذ الأرض فارغة. وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يكلف بالقلع ولكنه بالخيار إن شاء أخذها بالثمن وقيمة البناء والغرس وإن شاء ترك وبه قال الإمام الشافعي ومالك لأنه ليس متعدياً في البناء والغرس لثبوت ملكه فيه بالشراء فلا يعامل بأحكام العدوان فصار كالموهوب له والمشتري شراء فاسداً عند الإمام، وكما إذا زرعها المشتري فإن كل واحد منهم لا يكلف بالقلع لتصرفه في ملكه وهذا لأن ضرر الشفيع بالزام قيمة البناء والغرس أهون من ضرر المشتري بالقلع لأن الشفيع يحصل له بمقابلة الثمن عوضان وهو البناء والغرس فلا يعد ضرراً، ولم يحصل للمشتري بمقابلة القلع شيء فكان الأول أهون فكان أولى بالتحمل. ووجه ظاهر الرواية أنه بنى في محل تعلق به حق متأكد لغيره من غير تسليط منه فينتقض كالراهن إذا بنى في المرهون ولهذا تنتقض جميع تصرفات المشتري حتى الوقف والمسجد والمقبرة بخلاف الموهوب على قول أبي حنيفة والمشتري شراء فاسداً لأنه فعل بتسليط من المالك ولهذا لا ينتقض تصرفهما. وفي الزرع القياس أن يقلع إلا أننا استحسناً ولذا قلنا لا يقلع لأن له نهاية، وليس على الشفيع كبير ضرر بالتأخير لأنه يترك بأجرته. فإن قلت: الاسترداد عندهما بعد البناء فإن جواز الاسترداد دينا في أنه لا يكلف القلع بل يقتضي القلع كما في الشفيع قلت: يجوز أن يكون مراده بقوله والمشتري شراء فساداً احتجاج من أبي يوسف عن أبي حنيفة بمذهب أبي حنيفة كما أفصح به صاحب غاية البيان وهذا بعيد والأوجه أن يقال : إن لأبي يوسف في البناء بعد الشراء الفاسد القول المذكور والثاني كما قال الإمام ذكره في الإيضاح . قيد بما ذكر احترازاً عن الزخرفة وفي قاضيخان ولو اشترى الرجل داراً وزخرفها بالنقوش شيء كثير كان للشفيع الخيار إن شاء أخذها وأعطاه ما زاد فيها وإن شاء ترك اهـ. قال في المحيط : لأن نقص صفته لا يمكن وفيه نظر لأن المشتري إذا بنى على الدار المشفوعة كان أن ينقض البناء ويأخذ الدار ويعطيه ما زاد فيها وأجيب بأن البناء إذا قلع له قيمة في الجملة بخلاف الزخرفة. قوله أو بنى أو غرس مثال وليس بقيد لما في المحيط : ولو أن المشتري زرعها رطبة أو كرما يؤمر بقلعه كالبناء ..
He (the author) said: 'And the value of the building and the planting, if the buyer built or planted, or forced his removal) meaning: If the buyer built or planted in the preempted land, then the shafee is decreed the shuf'a, the shafee has the choice: if he wishes, he takes it (the land) for the price plus the value of the building and planting when uprooted; if he wishes, he forces the buyer to remove them and takes the land empty. It is reported from Abu Yusuf that he does not force removal, but he has the choice: if he wishes, he takes it for the price and the value of the building and planting, and if he wishes, he leaves it. Imam al-Shafi'i and Malik said the same. This is because the buyer is not a wrongdoer in building/planting as his ownership is established by purchase, so he is not treated under the rules of aggression; thus he is like the donee and the buyer in a void purchase according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), and like if the buyer planted crops, for each of them is not forced to remove because he acted in his property. And this is because the harm to the shafee by imposing the value of the building/planting is lighter than the harm to the buyer by removal, because the shafee receives in exchange for the price two compensations: the building and planting, so it is not considered harm, while the buyer receives nothing in exchange for removal, so the former is lighter and more worthy of being borne. The proof of the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) is that he (the buyer) built in a place where a firm right of another is attached without permission, so his action is nullified, like a mortgagor who builds on the mortgaged property; for this reason all the buyer's disposals are nullified, including endowment, mosque, and graveyard, contrary to the donee according to Abu Hanifa and the buyer in a void purchase, because they acted with the owner's permission, so their disposals are not nullified. As for planting, the analogy is that it should be removed, but we practised istihsan, therefore we say it is not removed because it has an end, and there is no great harm to the shafee by delay, as he leaves it for rent. If you say: The rescission (istirdad) according to them after building: the permissibility of rescission indicates that he is not forced to remove but rather removal is required, as for the shafee. I say: It is possible that his saying 'the buyer in a void purchase' is a citation of Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa about the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, as the author of Ghayat al-Bayan clarified, but this is far-fetched, and the sounder view is that Abu Yusuf has two opinions regarding building after a void purchase, the second being as the Imam said, mentioned in al-Idah. He restricted it to what is mentioned (building/planting) to exclude decoration (zakhrafa). In Qadikhan: If a man bought a house and decorated it with many ornaments, the shafee has the choice: if he wishes, he takes it and gives what increased, and if he wishes, he leaves it. End. Al-Muhit said: Because the deficiency of its description cannot be (remedied). There is some doubt here, because if the buyer built on the preempted house, he can demolish the building and take the house and give what increased. The answer is that when the building is removed, it has some value overall, unlike decoration. His saying 'or built or planted' is an example and not a restriction, as in al-Muhit: If the buyer planted it with grass or grapevines, he is ordered to remove them like the building.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage discusses a key dispute regarding the shafee's rights when the buyer has made improvements to the preempted property before the shuf'a is claimed. The core issue: can the shafee force the buyer to demolish buildings or uproot trees, or must the shafee pay for them? The text presents the Hanafi position (zahir al-riwaya) attributed to Abu Hanifa: the shafee has the option to either take the property with the improvements by paying their value when removed (qima mql-u'an) or to force the buyer to remove them at his own cost. The opposing view, attributed to Abu Yusuf (and agreed by Shafi'i and Malik), is that the shafee cannot force removal; he can only take the property by paying the value of the improvements, or leave it. The reasoning for Abu Yusuf's view: the buyer is not a wrongdoer because he owned the property legally, so his construction is permissible; forcing removal would cause him more harm than the shafee would suffer by paying for the improvements. The Hanafi response (supporting the first view) uses an analogy: the buyer built on land that had a right attached (the shuf'a right), without permission of the right-holder, similar to a mortgagor building on mortgaged property—such acts are nullified. However, a donee or buyer in a void purchase (where the owner gave permission) is not nullified. For crops (planting), the Hanafi school uses istihsan to not force removal because crops have a fixed term and the shafee can take rent, avoiding immediate harm. The text further clarifies that decoration (zakhrafa) is different: the shafee cannot force removal of decorative paintings because they have no value when removed, so he must pay for the increase in value. This is a nuanced area of Hanafi jurisprudence balancing the rights of the shafee and the buyer.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The well-known transmitted view of the Hanafi school, primarily from Abu Hanifa, recorded in the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The shafee's option to choose between taking property with improvements at a cost or forcing removal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir al-riwaya (Abu Hanifa), what are the shafee's options if the buyer built on the preempted property?
A) Only take the property by paying the value of the building
B) Force the buyer to remove the building or take it by paying the removal value
C) Only leave the property
D) Take the property without paying anything

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives the shafee the powerful right to force removal of buyer's improvements, based on qiyas with the mortgagor, unless an istihsan exception applies (crops) or the improvement lacks removable value (decoration).

Limit on Shafee's Recovery When Property Is Reclaimed
Ruling on shafee's recourse after building and reclamation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the shafee's recourse when his buildings are reclaimed, explain the distinction between the shafee and the buyer, and identify Abu Yusuf's differing view.

Core Idea
The shafee recovers only the purchase price, not improvement value, because he was not deceived by the seller.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a crucial nuance in shuf'a (preemption) law. When a shafee successfully preempts a property, builds on it, and later a true owner reclaims the land, what can the shafee recover? The text provides the relied-upon Hanafi position and explains the rational distinction between the shafee and the original buyer. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly applying the principles of ghurur (deception) and taslit (authorization) in property transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage states that if the shafee removes the buildings after the property is reclaimed, he recovers only the purchase price (al-thaman). Determine whether this applies when the shafee takes from the buyer or from the seller.
The text explicitly mentions both scenarios: the shafee claims from the buyer or the seller.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that the shafee recovers the value of the buildings as well, analogous to the buyer. Identify the opposing view and its basis.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
3
Understand the rational basis
The text distinguishes the shafee from the buyer: the buyer is maghrur (deceived) by the seller and is musallat (authorized) to take possession, whereas the shafee is not. Analyse how this distinction justifies the different outcomes.
The key terms are ghurur and taslit – these form the dalil aqli.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text indicates that the dominant view (zahir al-riwaya) is the first: the shafee recovers only the price. Confirm that this is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
Al-Marghinani typically adopts the view that aligns with the majority of the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وإن قلعهما الشفيع فاستحقت رجع بالثمن فقط) يعني أن الشفيع إذا أخذ الأرض بالشفعة فبنى أو غرس ثم استحقت فكلف المستحق الشفيع بالقلع فقلع البناء والغرس رجع الشفيع على المشتري إن أخذها منه أو على البائع إن أخذها منه بالثمن ولا يرجع بقيمة البناء والغرس وعن أبي يوسف أنه يرجع بذلك كالمشتري. والفرق بينه وبين المشتري أن المشتري مغرور ومن جهة البائع ومسلط عليه من جهته ولا غرور ولا تسليط
He (the author) said: 'And if the shafee removes them and the property is reclaimed, he recovers only the purchase price.' Meaning that if the shafee takes the land by shuf'a, then builds or plants, then the land is reclaimed (by a rightful claimant), and the claimant forces the shafee to remove the building and planting, the shafee recovers from the buyer (if he claimed from him) or from the seller (if he claimed from him) the purchase price, but he does not recover the value of the building and planting. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he recovers that as well, like the buyer. The difference between him and the buyer is that the buyer is deceived (maghrur) and from the side of the seller and is authorized by him, while there is no deception nor authorization here.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario: after the shafee successfully takes the property through preemption and then builds on it, a third party (mustahiqq) comes and proves ownership, forcing the shafee to demolish the buildings. In such a case, the shafee can only claim back the purchase price he paid (to the buyer or seller), not the value of the buildings he built. The reasoning: the shafee was not deceived (ghurur) by the seller like the original buyer would be. The original buyer was misled by the seller's representation of title; the shafee enters into the contract with knowledge (or assumption) of the title, so he bears the loss of his improvements. Abu Yusuf disagrees, equating the shafee to the buyer in terms of recovering the value of improvements, but the dominant view (zahir al-riwaya) is that the shafee does not. This ruling reinforces the principle that the shafee takes the property subject to its existing condition; if he adds improvements and the title fails, his recourse is limited. It also highlights the distinction between the original buyer (who was 'deceived' by the seller) and the shafee (who is not deceived, as he takes from the buyer).

Key Terms
مغرور (maghrur): One who is deceived or misled in a transaction, typically by the seller's representation of ownership.
تسليط (taslit): Authorization or empowerment given by the seller to the buyer to take possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the shafee builds on the property he took by shuf'a, and then the true owner reclaims it, what can the shafee recover?
A) The purchase price only
B) The purchase price plus the value of the building
C) Only the value of the building
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The shafee's limited recovery to the purchase price underscores the principle that he assumes the risk of improvements, unlike the buyer who relied on the seller's misrepresentation.

Shuf'ah: Buildings and Trees – When They Collapse or Are Demolished
Ruling with proof on taking land with fixtures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a pre-emptor takes land at full price if fixtures perish naturally, and at a proportionate price if the purchaser demolishes them.

Core Idea
Fixtures are subsidiary to land; their destruction by nature does not reduce price, but intentional demolition does.

This passage from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the quantum of what a pre-emptor (shafi') receives when exercising shuf'ah. The key is whether the building or trees perished without human action (pre-emptor pays full price) or were demolished by the purchaser (price reduced). The principle of taba'iyyah (subsidiarity) governs the classification of fixtures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The matn gives two main scenarios: (1) building collapses or trees dry up without human action; (2) purchaser demolishes the building.
The commentary adds detail about remaining debris.
2
Apply the rule for natural collapse
If fixtures perish naturally, they are considered part of the land; pre-emptor pays full price because no separate value was allocated.
Analogous to Murabaha sale without disclosure.
3
Apply the rule for purchaser demolition
If purchaser demolishes, the building becomes an independent object; pre-emptor pays only the land's share of the price.
The demolished material belongs to the purchaser.
4
Consider partial remaining debris
If any debris remains and the purchaser takes it, a proportion of the price must be subtracted.
Price is divided based on value at contract and value of debris at taking.
5
Note the third-party demolition
Demolition by a third party is treated like demolition by the purchaser.
From al-Tatarkhaniyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة باب طلب الشفعة وبكل الثمن أن خربت الدار وجف الشجر وبحصة العرصة أن نقض المشتري البناء والنقض له وبثمرها إن ابتاع أرضاً ونخلاً وثمراً أو أثمر في يده . قال من يده رحمه للشفيع من جهة المشتري ولا البائع لأن الشفيع أخذها منه جبراً، ونظيره الجارية المأسورة إذا استردها المالك القديم من مالكها الجديد بقيمتها أو بالثمن فاستولدها ثم استحقت وضمن قيمة الولد رجع عليه بما دفع له من القيمة أو الثمن ولا يرجع بقيمة الولد لأنه لم يغره بخلاف ما لو كان مشترياً حيث يرجع بهما على البائع لأنه مغرور من جهته. الله : وبكل الثمن أن خربت الدار وجف الشجر) يعني لو اشترى أرضاً فيها بناء أو غرس فانهدم البناء من غير صنع أحد يأخذها الشفيع بكل الثمن ولا يسقط من الثمن شيء لأنهما تابعان للأرض يدخلان في بيعها من غير ذكر فلا يقابلها شيء من الثمن ولهذا يبيعها في هذه الحالة مرابحة من غير بيان بخلاف ما إذا تلف بعض الأرض بغرق حيث يسقط من الثمن بحصته لأن الغالب بعض الأصل هذا إذا انهدم البناء ولم يبق له نقض ولا من الشجر شيء من حطب أو خشب، وأما إذا بقي شيء من ذلك وأخذه المشتري فلا بد من سقوط بعض الثمن بحصته ذلك لأنه عين مال قائم بقي يحبس عند المشتري فيكون له حصة من الثمن فيقسم الثمن على قيمة الدار يوم العقد وعلى قيمة النقض يوم الأخذ. قيد بقوله «حق الشجر» ليخرج الثمر إذا هلك من غير صنع . قال في التتارخانية : ولو هلك الثمر من غير صنع أحد ولم يبق منه شيء سقط حصته من الثمن بخلاف البناء وسيأتي ما يخالفه. قال رحمه الله : (وبحصة العرصة أن نقض المشتري (البناء يعني يأخذ الشفيع العرصة بحصتها من الثمن إن نقض المشتري البناء لأنه صار مقصوداً بالإتلاف ويقابله شيء من الثمن فيقسم الثمن على قيمة الأرض والبناء . يوم العقد، ونقض الأجنبي النياء كنقض المشتري. وفي التتارخانية : لو لم يهدم المشتري البناء ولكن باعه من غيره من غير إرضاء ثم حضر الشفيع فله أن ينقض البيع ويأخذ الكل وكذا النبات والنخل . قال رحمه الله : (والنقض له) يعني النقض للمشتري لأن الشفيع للمشتري لأن الشفيع إنما كان يأخذه بطريق التبعية للعرصة وقد زالت بالانفصال. رحمه الله : ( وبثمرها إن ابتاع أرضاً ونخلاً وثمراً أو أثمر في يده) يعني يأخذها الشفيع مع ثمرها إن كان المشتري اشترى الأرض مع الثمر بأن شرطه في البيع أو أثمر عند المشتري بعد راء لأن الثمر لا يدخل في البيع إلا بالشرط بخلاف النخل والقياس أن لا يكون له أخذ الثمر لعدم التبعية كالمتاع الموضوع فيها. وجه الاستحسان أن الاتصال خلقة صار تبعاً من وجه ولا يتولد من البيع فيسري إليه الحق الثابت في الأصل كالمبيعة إذا ولدت قبل القبض فإن المشتري يملك الولد تبعاً للأم كذا هنا. وفي الخانية : لو اشترى قرية فيها أشجار ونخل فقطع المشتري بعض الأشجار وهدم بعض البناء فحضر الشفيع يأخذ الأرض وما لم يقطع من الأشجار وما لم يهدم من البناء وليس له أن يأخذها بالشفعة ويقسم الثمن على قيمة البناء قال الشراء وإن جذه المشتري سقط حصته من الثمن قال رحمه الله : (وإن جذه المشتري سقط حصته من الثمن يعني في الفصل الأول وهو ما اشتراها بثمرها بالشرط فكان له فيسقط من الثمن بحصته، وإن هلك بآفة سماوية فكذلك لأنه لما دخل في البيع صار أصلاً فسقط حصته من الثمن بفواته . وأما في الأصل الثاني فيأخذ الأرض والنخل بجميع الثمن لأن الثمن لم يكن موجوداً عند العقد فلا يقابله شيء من الثمن، وكان أبو يوسف يقول أولاً إنه يحط من الثمن في الفصل الثاني لأن حال المشتري مع الشفيع كحال البائع مع المشتري قبل القبض. ولو أكل البائع الثمر الحادث بعد القبض سقط حصته من الثمن فكذا هنا ثم رجع إلى ما ذكر في أنه لا يسقط شيء من الثمن لأن الشفيع يأخذ بما قام على المشتري وهو قائم عليه المبيع بدون الثمن بجميع الثمن بخلاف ما إذا كانت موجودة عند العقد لأنه دخل في البيع قصداً، وبخلاف الحادث عند البائع قبل القبض لأنه حدث على ملك المشتري فيكون له حصة من الثمن بالاستهلاك، وليس للشفيع أن يأخذ الثمن بعد الجذاذ في الفصلين لزوال التبعية بالانفصال قبل الأخذ والله تعالى أعلم .
The Book of Pre-emption, Chapter: Seeking Pre-emption: And with the full price if the house collapses and the trees dry up, and with the share of the plot if the purchaser demolishes the building and the demolished material belongs to him, and with its fruit if he buys land and palm trees and fruit, or fruit appears in his hand. He said: from his hand, may Allah have mercy on him, for the pre-emptor from the side of the purchaser and not the seller because the pre-emptor takes it from him compulsorily. Its analogy is the captured slave girl when the original owner reclaims her from the new owner by her value or by the price, then she gives birth, then she is claimed and he is made liable for the value of the child; he recovers from him what he paid of value or price, but does not recover the value of the child because he did not deceive him, contrary to the case of a purchaser, where he recovers both from the seller because he is deceived by him. Allah: and with the full price if the house collapses and the trees dry up) meaning if he buys land upon which there is a building or plantation, and the building collapses without any human action, the pre-emptor takes it with the full price and nothing of the price drops because they are subsidiary to the land and enter its sale without mention, so nothing of the price corresponds to them; for this reason he sells it in this case by Murabaha without disclosure, contrary to the case when part of the land perishes by drowning, where a share of the price drops because the part is from the original. This is when the building collapses and no debris remains, nor from the trees any firewood or timber. But if any of that remains and the purchaser takes it, then a part of the price must drop correspondingly, because it is existing property left impounded with the purchaser, so he has a share of the price; so the price is divided based on the value of the house on the day of contract and the value of the debris on the day of taking. He restricted by his saying 'the right of the tree' to exclude fruit if it perishes without human action. He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If the fruit perishes without human action and nothing of it remains, its share of the price drops, contrary to the building, and something contradictory will come later. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And with the share of the plot if the purchaser demolishes the building) meaning the pre-emptor takes the plot with its share of the price if the purchaser demolishes the building because it became an object of destruction intentionally and something of the price corresponds to it; so the price is divided based on the value of the land and the building on the day of contract. And demolition by a third party is like demolition by the purchaser. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: If the purchaser did not demolish the building but sold it to someone else without satisfaction, then the pre-emptor appears, he may annul the sale and take all, and likewise with plants and palm trees. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the debris belongs to him) meaning the debris belongs to the purchaser because the pre-emptor only used to take it by way of being subsidiary to the plot, and that subsister has ceased by separation. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And with its fruit if he buys land and palm trees and fruit, or fruit appears in his hand) meaning the pre-emptor takes it with its fruit if the purchaser bought the land with the fruit by stipulating it in the sale, or fruit appeared with the purchaser after purchase, because fruit does not enter the sale except by stipulation, unlike the palm trees. The analogy is that he should not have the right to take the fruit due to lack of subsidy, like goods placed therein. The basis of istihsan is that connection by creation makes it subsidiary in some respect, and it does not generate from the sale, so the right established in the principal extends to it, like a sold animal if it gives birth before delivery, the purchaser owns the offspring as subsidiary to the mother. So here. In al-Khaniyya: If he buys a village containing trees and palm trees, and the purchaser cuts some trees and demolishes some buildings, then the pre-emptor appears, he takes the land and what remains uncut of trees and what remains undemolished of buildings; he does not have the right to take it by pre-emption and divides the price based on the value of the building? He said: And if the purchaser cuts it, its share of the price drops. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if the purchaser cuts it, its share of the price drops) means in the first case, which is when he bought it with its fruit by stipulation, so it was his, so its share of the price drops. And if it perishes by a heavenly calamity, likewise, because when it entered the sale it became a principal, so its share of the price drops due to its loss. As for the second case, he takes the land and palm trees with the full price because the fruit was not present at the time of contract, so nothing of the price corresponds to it. Abu Yusuf first said that a deduction is made from the price in the second case because the situation of the purchaser with the pre-emptor is like the situation of the seller with the purchaser before delivery. If the seller had eaten the fruit that appeared after delivery, its share of the price would drop, so here too. Then he retracted to what is mentioned, that nothing of the price drops because the pre-emptor takes on whatever basis the purchaser took, and the sold item without the fruit stands upon the full price, contrary to when it was present at the contract because it entered the sale intentionally, and contrary to the fruit that appears with the seller before delivery because it occurs on the ownership of the purchaser, so he has a share of the price due to consumption. And the pre-emptor does not have the right to take the fruit after picking in both cases because of the loss of subsidy by separation before taking. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the detailed rulings of what a pre-emptor (shafi') takes when he exercises his right of shuf'ah. The matn states several scenarios: (1) If the house collapses or trees dry up without human action, the pre-emptor takes the land at the full price because buildings and trees are fixtures and are included in the sale of land without separate consideration. (2) If the purchaser demolishes the building, the pre-emptor takes only the land at a proportion of the price corresponding to the land's value, because the building was intentionally destroyed and its value is no longer part of the sale; the demolished material belongs to the purchaser. (3) If the land was bought with trees and fruit (by stipulation) or fruit grew in the purchaser's hand, the pre-emptor takes the fruit along with the land. The commentary expands with examples and analogies. It also discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf initially held that if fruit grew after purchase (without being stipulated), a proportion of the price should be deducted because the purchaser consumed it, but he retracted and agreed with Abu Hanifa that no deduction is made because the fruit was not part of the sale; the pre-emptor takes the land at the same price the purchaser paid, ignoring the fruit. The passage also references the Tatarkhaniyya and Khaniyya for additional details. The legal principle is that the pre-emptor steps into the shoes of the purchaser, taking the property as it was at the time of sale, with all fixtures, but if the purchaser has destroyed or severed something, the price is adjusted accordingly.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to pre-empt the sale of property by taking it from the buyer for the same price.
تبعية (taba'iyyah): The legal principle that a subsidiary item (like a fixture) follows the principal (land) in ownership and sale.
استحسان (istihsan): Equitable preference; a departure from strict analogy due to a stronger legal basis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the pre-emptor take if the purchaser demolishes the building before the pre-emptor appears?
A) The land and the demolished building material.
B) The land only, at a proportion of the price corresponding to the land's value.
C) The land at the full price.
D) Nothing, because the property has changed.

Key Takeaway
Fixtures are subsidiary; natural destruction does not reduce price, but intentional demolition by the purchaser does, with the material belonging to him.

Shuf'ah: Fruit – When Included or Grown After Purchase
Ruling with proof on taking fruit in shuf'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on whether a pre-emptor takes fruit along with the land, and determine when a price adjustment occurs, including the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
If fruit was stipulated in the sale, it is taken with land; if it grew after, it is also taken, but no price reduction for post-sale growth.

This section of the passage focuses on fruit attached to trees on land subject to shuf'ah. The matn states that if the purchaser bought the land 'with its fruit' (by stipulation), or if fruit grew after purchase, the pre-emptor takes the fruit as well. The commentary explains the reasoning, including an istihsan based on connection by creation, and records Abu Yusuf's initial disagreement and retraction.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish two cases
Case 1: Fruit was included in the sale by stipulation. Case 2: Fruit grew after purchase without stipulation.
The ruling differs for each.
2
Case 1: Fruit in sale
If fruit was stipulated, the pre-emptor takes it with land. If the fruit is then cut (by purchaser) or perishes, a proportion of the price drops.
Because the fruit was part of the sale (principal).
3
Case 2: Fruit grew after sale
Hanafi position: pre-emptor takes the land and fruit at the full price paid by purchaser; no reduction. Abu Yusuf initially held a deduction should be made, but he retracted.
The reasoning: the fruit was not part of the sale, so the pre-emptor steps into the purchaser's shoes as the property was at contract.
4
Understand the istihsan
The usual analogy (qiyas) would treat fruit like goods (mata') not subsidiary, but istihsan rules that connection by creation makes it subsidiary in this context.
Analogy with a sold animal giving birth before delivery.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if fruit is already picked (cut) before the pre-emptor acts, in Case 2 the pre-emptor does not take the fruit and no deduction is made.
In Case 1, deduction is made.
