The Book of Pre-emption, Chapter: Seeking Pre-emption: And with the full price if the house collapses and the trees dry up, and with the share of the plot if the purchaser demolishes the building and the demolished material belongs to him, and with its fruit if he buys land and palm trees and fruit, or fruit appears in his hand. He said: from his hand, may Allah have mercy on him, for the pre-emptor from the side of the purchaser and not the seller because the pre-emptor takes it from him compulsorily. Its analogy is the captured slave girl when the original owner reclaims her from the new owner by her value or by the price, then she gives birth, then she is claimed and he is made liable for the value of the child; he recovers from him what he paid of value or price, but does not recover the value of the child because he did not deceive him, contrary to the case of a purchaser, where he recovers both from the seller because he is deceived by him. Allah: and with the full price if the house collapses and the trees dry up) meaning if he buys land upon which there is a building or plantation, and the building collapses without any human action, the pre-emptor takes it with the full price and nothing of the price drops because they are subsidiary to the land and enter its sale without mention, so nothing of the price corresponds to them; for this reason he sells it in this case by Murabaha without disclosure, contrary to the case when part of the land perishes by drowning, where a share of the price drops because the part is from the original. This is when the building collapses and no debris remains, nor from the trees any firewood or timber. But if any of that remains and the purchaser takes it, then a part of the price must drop correspondingly, because it is existing property left impounded with the purchaser, so he has a share of the price; so the price is divided based on the value of the house on the day of contract and the value of the debris on the day of taking. He restricted by his saying 'the right of the tree' to exclude fruit if it perishes without human action. He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If the fruit perishes without human action and nothing of it remains, its share of the price drops, contrary to the building, and something contradictory will come later. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And with the share of the plot if the purchaser demolishes the building) meaning the pre-emptor takes the plot with its share of the price if the purchaser demolishes the building because it became an object of destruction intentionally and something of the price corresponds to it; so the price is divided based on the value of the land and the building on the day of contract. And demolition by a third party is like demolition by the purchaser. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: If the purchaser did not demolish the building but sold it to someone else without satisfaction, then the pre-emptor appears, he may annul the sale and take all, and likewise with plants and palm trees. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the debris belongs to him) meaning the debris belongs to the purchaser because the pre-emptor only used to take it by way of being subsidiary to the plot, and that subsister has ceased by separation. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And with its fruit if he buys land and palm trees and fruit, or fruit appears in his hand) meaning the pre-emptor takes it with its fruit if the purchaser bought the land with the fruit by stipulating it in the sale, or fruit appeared with the purchaser after purchase, because fruit does not enter the sale except by stipulation, unlike the palm trees. The analogy is that he should not have the right to take the fruit due to lack of subsidy, like goods placed therein. The basis of istihsan is that connection by creation makes it subsidiary in some respect, and it does not generate from the sale, so the right established in the principal extends to it, like a sold animal if it gives birth before delivery, the purchaser owns the offspring as subsidiary to the mother. So here. In al-Khaniyya: If he buys a village containing trees and palm trees, and the purchaser cuts some trees and demolishes some buildings, then the pre-emptor appears, he takes the land and what remains uncut of trees and what remains undemolished of buildings; he does not have the right to take it by pre-emption and divides the price based on the value of the building? He said: And if the purchaser cuts it, its share of the price drops. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if the purchaser cuts it, its share of the price drops) means in the first case, which is when he bought it with its fruit by stipulation, so it was his, so its share of the price drops. And if it perishes by a heavenly calamity, likewise, because when it entered the sale it became a principal, so its share of the price drops due to its loss. As for the second case, he takes the land and palm trees with the full price because the fruit was not present at the time of contract, so nothing of the price corresponds to it. Abu Yusuf first said that a deduction is made from the price in the second case because the situation of the purchaser with the pre-emptor is like the situation of the seller with the purchaser before delivery. If the seller had eaten the fruit that appeared after delivery, its share of the price would drop, so here too. Then he retracted to what is mentioned, that nothing of the price drops because the pre-emptor takes on whatever basis the purchaser took, and the sold item without the fruit stands upon the full price, contrary to when it was present at the contract because it entered the sale intentionally, and contrary to the fruit that appears with the seller before delivery because it occurs on the ownership of the purchaser, so he has a share of the price due to consumption. And the pre-emptor does not have the right to take the fruit after picking in both cases because of the loss of subsidy by separation before taking. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the detailed rulings of what a pre-emptor (shafi') takes when he exercises his right of shuf'ah. The matn states several scenarios: (1) If the house collapses or trees dry up without human action, the pre-emptor takes the land at the full price because buildings and trees are fixtures and are included in the sale of land without separate consideration. (2) If the purchaser demolishes the building, the pre-emptor takes only the land at a proportion of the price corresponding to the land's value, because the building was intentionally destroyed and its value is no longer part of the sale; the demolished material belongs to the purchaser. (3) If the land was bought with trees and fruit (by stipulation) or fruit grew in the purchaser's hand, the pre-emptor takes the fruit along with the land. The commentary expands with examples and analogies. It also discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf initially held that if fruit grew after purchase (without being stipulated), a proportion of the price should be deducted because the purchaser consumed it, but he retracted and agreed with Abu Hanifa that no deduction is made because the fruit was not part of the sale; the pre-emptor takes the land at the same price the purchaser paid, ignoring the fruit. The passage also references the Tatarkhaniyya and Khaniyya for additional details. The legal principle is that the pre-emptor steps into the shoes of the purchaser, taking the property as it was at the time of sale, with all fixtures, but if the purchaser has destroyed or severed something, the price is adjusted accordingly.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to pre-empt the sale of property by taking it from the buyer for the same price.
تبعية (taba'iyyah): The legal principle that a subsidiary item (like a fixture) follows the principal (land) in ownership and sale.
استحسان (istihsan): Equitable preference; a departure from strict analogy due to a stronger legal basis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the final Hanafi ruling when fruit grows on purchased land after the sale, without stipulation, and the pre-emptor appears?
A) The pre-emptor takes the fruit and pays a reduced price.
B) The pre-emptor takes the land with the fruit and pays the full price.
C) The pre-emptor does not take the fruit and pays a reduced price.
D) The pre-emptor does not take the fruit but pays the full price.

Key Takeaway
Fruit attached by creation is subsidiary; if not stipulated, it is taken with land at full price with no deduction.

General Conditions for Shuf'ah: Real Estate and Consideration
Defining the scope of shuf'ah: it applies only to real estate acquired for valuable consideration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two essential conditions for shuf'ah in the Hanafi school and distinguish between eligible and ineligible properties.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah applies only to real estate acquired through a valuable consideration — not to gifts, movables, or non-wealth exchanges.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq begins the chapter on shuf'ah, outlining its scope. The Hanafi school restricts shuf'ah to real estate ('aqar) that was transferred in exchange for a valuable consideration ('iwad) that is wealth (mal). Understanding these conditions is essential for any muamalat practitioner, as they determine when a co-owner can assert the right of pre-emption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property condition
The text states shuf'ah is only wajib in 'aqar (real estate). Determine what qualifies as 'aqar: land, buildings, palm trees attached to land, and even indivisible property like wells and roads according to the Hanafis.
Shafi'i disagrees on indivisible property — see dispute.
2
Identify the consideration condition
The acquisition must be 'bi 'iwad' (by a consideration) and that 'iwad must be mal (wealth). Exclude gifts (hibah), mahr, khul', sulh for homicide, and manumission.
Non-wealth considerations do not trigger shuf'ah.
3
Analyze the madhhab dispute
Hanafi holds shuf'ah applies to all real estate, even indivisible; Shafi'i restricts to divisible property. The difference stems from the underlying purpose: preventing harm from bad neighborliness (Hanafi) vs. cost of division (Shafi'i).
4
Apply the conditions to examples
List transactions: sale of a house (eligible), gift of land (ineligible), sale of a movable ship (ineligible), sale of a share in a well (eligible in Hanafi).
Always check both conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يجب فيه الشفعة وما لا يجب إنما تجب الشفعة في عقار ملك بعوض هو مال لا في عرض وفلك وبناء ونخل بيعا والأرض. وذكر تفصيل ما تجب فيه الشفعة وما لا تجب بعد ذكر نفس الوجوب مجملاً لأن التفصيل بعد الإجمال أوقع في النفس؛ كذا في العناية. قال رحمه الله : (إنما تجب الشفعة في عقار ملك بعوض هو مال) قوله «في عقار يتناول ما يقسم وما لا يقسم. وقال الشافعي : لا تجب فيما لا يقسم كالبئر والرحا والحمام والنهر والطريق وهذا مبني على أصل عنده وهو أن الشفعة تجب لدفع ضرر أجرة القسام عنده وعندنا لدفع ضرر سوء العشرة. واحترز بقوله بعوض عما إذا ملك بالهبة فإن الشفعة لا تجب فيها ، وقوله هو مال عما إذا ملك بعوض غير مال كالمهر والخلع والصلح عن دم عمد والعتق فإن الشفعة لا تجب في هذه الأشياء على ما بينه قريباً، والعقار لغة الضيعة، وقيل ماله أصل من دار وضيعة نقله الإمام الطرزي. ونقل الشراح هنا العقار كل ماله أصل من دار وضيعة .اهـ. فهو مطابق للتفسير الثاني. ونقل الجوهري في فصل العين من باب الراء : العقار بالفتح الأرض والضياع والنخل ومنه قولهم ماله دار ولا عقار والجمع ضياع. وفي فصل الضاد من باب العين: الضيعة
Chapter: What requires pre-emption and what does not. Pre-emption is only required in real estate owned by a consideration that is wealth, not in movable property, nor a ship, nor a building, nor palm trees sold separately (as movables), nor land. And a detailed explanation of what requires pre-emption and what does not after mentioning the requirement in summary, because detail after summary makes a greater impression; as in al-‘Inaya. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Pre-emption is only required in real estate owned by a consideration that is wealth). His saying “in real estate” includes what is divisible and what is indivisible. Al-Shafi‘i said: It is not required in what is indivisible, like a well, a mill, a bathhouse, a river, and a road. This is based on a principle with him: that pre-emption is required to repel the harm of the cost of division; with us, it is to repel the harm of bad neighborliness. He restricted by his saying “by a consideration” against the case of ownership by gift, for then pre-emption is not required. And his saying “that is wealth” against the case of ownership by a consideration that is not wealth, like mahr, khul‘, sulh for intentional homicide, and manumission, for pre-emption is not required in these things, as will be explained shortly. Real estate linguistically is al-diy‘ah (estate), and it is said: anything that has a root from a house or estate, as transmitted by Imam al-Tarzi. The commentators here transmitted: real estate is everything that has a root from a house or estate. So it corresponds to the second interpretation. Al-Jawhari transmitted: “al-‘aqar” with a fatha means land, estates, and palm trees; hence their saying: he has neither a house nor real estate; the plural is diya‘. In the section on dad from the chapter on ‘ayn: al-diy‘ah.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on the conditions for shuf'ah. The matn states that shuf'ah is only wajib (established) in real estate ('aqar) that is owned by a consideration ('iwad) that is wealth (mal). It explicitly excludes movable goods ('ard), ships (falk), buildings (bina'), and palm trees (nakhl) when sold separately, and land (ard) actually is included (the text says 'la fi...wa'ard' which is confusing; the matn likely means 'not in movables, ships, buildings, and palm trees sold as movables, but it is in land.' The commentary clarifies that 'aqar includes divisible and indivisible property, contrary to al-Shafi'i who excludes indivisible property. The basis of shuf'ah according to the Hanafis is to prevent harm from bad neighborliness, while Shafi‘i's basis is the cost of division. The condition 'bi 'iwad' excludes gifts (hibah). 'Huwa mal' excludes non-wealth considerations like mahr (dower in marriage), khul‘ (divorce by compensation), sulh for homicide, and manumission. The passage also gives the linguistic definition of 'aqar from authorities like al-Jawhari, confirming it means land and estates, including palm trees. The commentary emphasises that the matn's ruling is that shuf'ah does not apply to movable items or to property acquired without a valuable consideration.

Key Terms
عقار ('aqar): Real estate; property with a fixed location such as land, buildings, and palm trees attached to the land.
عوض ('iwad): Consideration; something given in exchange for ownership, like a price in a sale.
مال (mal): Wealth; property that has value in the shari'ah, such as dirhams, dinars, goods, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT eligible for shuf'ah according to the Hanafi school?
A) A house bought for 1000 dirhams
B) A piece of land received as a gift
C) A palm tree sold along with the land
D) An indivisible share in a shared well

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah in the Hanafi school requires both real estate and a valuable consideration (mal) for the right to arise.

Definition of 'Aqar and Exceptions to Shuf'a
Definition and exceptions of shuf'a in Hanafi fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'aqar (immovable property) and identify which transactions involving 'aqar are exempt from the right of shuf'a.

Core Idea
Shuf'a only applies to owned immovable property; not every sale triggers it.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi definition of 'aqar (immovable property) as it pertains to pre-emption (shuf'a). The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that shuf'a is not established in every sale of land or buildings; certain transactions — such as a house given as dower or a defective sale — are exempt. We will explore the boundaries of what constitutes 'aqar and the specific exceptions that prevent shuf'a.

Guided Path
1
Define 'Aqar
Understand that 'aqar includes land, estates, date palms, and anything fixed to the ground. Movable items are not 'aqar.
Fixed items like the lower millstone are 'aqar; the upper stone is not.
2
List exceptions from the matn
Identify the transactions that do not give rise to shuf'a: sale without land, dower, rent, khulu', blood money, emancipation compensation, gift, sale with option, defective sale.
These are money or non-monetary transfers that do not trigger pre-emption.
3
Apply the ownership condition
Shuf'a only applies to privately owned land. It does not apply to waqf, mosque property, or lands granted by the Imam for cultivation.
The lack of full ownership is a fundamental bar.

Source Text

كتاب الشفعة باب ما يجب فيه الشفعة وما لا يجب هو بلا عرصة ودار جعلت مهراً أو أجرة أو بدل خلق أو بدل صلح عن دم عمد أو عوض عتق أو وهبت بلا عوض مشروط وإن بيعت بخيار البائع أو بيعت فاسدا ما لم العقار اهـ. وفي كلامه اختلال لأنه فسر العقار أولاً بما يشمل الأقسام الثلاثة الأرض والضياع والنخل، ثم فسر الضيعة بالعقار فلزم تفسير الأخص بالأعم كما ترى. وفي المحيط : ويدخل في الحمام ما كان مركباً في بنيانه دون المنفصل كالقصعة، ويدخل في الرحا الحجر الأسفل دون الأعلى لأنه مبني في الأرض، ولو اشترى أجمة فيها قصب وسمك يوجد بلا صيد استحق الأجمة والقصب بالشفعة دون السمك لأنه منقول والقصب يشعب في الأرض. وفي التتارخانية وإنما تجب في الأراضي التي تملك رقابها حتى لا تجب في الأراضي التي جازها الإمام للمسلمين يدفعها بزراعة، وإنما تجب لحق الملك في الأراضي لو بيعت دار بجنبها دار الوقف فلا شفعة للواقف ولا للمتولي لعدم الملك؛ كذا في المحيط وغيره. وفي السراجية رجل له دار في أرض الوقف فلا شفعة له، ولو باع عمارته فلا شفعة لجاره. وفي التجريد ولو جعل داره مسجداً وأفرزه وجعل بابه إلى الطريق فبیعت دار إلى جنب المسجد لم يكن للواقف ولا للمتولي شفعة لعدم الملك. وفي المحيط وغيره : ما لا يجوز بيعه في العقارات كالأوقاف والحانوت المسبل فلا شفعة في ذلك عند من يرى جواز الوقف. وفي المبسوط : لو اشترى أرضاً فيها شجر صغار فأثمرت أو فيها زرع فأدرك فللشفيع أن يأخذ ذلك بجميع الثمر لاتصاله بالأرض اهـ.
The Book of Pre-emption, Chapter on What Requires Pre-emption and What Does Not. It (the property that does not require pre-emption) is: without land, a house given as dower or rent or as consideration for khulu' or as settlement for intentional killing or as compensation for emancipation, or given as a gift without compensation even if conditional, or sold with a condition of option for the seller, or sold in a defective contract, as long as the real estate is not [sold with it]. In his speech there is confusion because he first defined 'real estate' as including three categories: land, estates, and date palms; then he defined 'estate' as real estate, resulting in defining the more specific by the more general, as you see. In al-Muhit: Included in the bathhouse is what is fixed in its structure, not what is detachable like a bowl. Included in the mill is the lower stone, not the upper, because it is built into the ground. If one buys a thicket containing reeds and fish found without hunting, the thicket and reeds are subject to pre-emption but not the fish because it is movable, while the reeds are rooted in the ground. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: Pre-emption is only due in lands whose ownership is possessed, so it is not due in lands that the Imam has given to Muslims for cultivation. It is due for the right of ownership. If a house next to a waqf house is sold, there is no pre-emption for the waqf founder or the trustee due to lack of ownership. Similarly in al-Muhit and others. In al-Sirajiyya: A man has a house on waqf land — no pre-emption for him. If he sells his building, no pre-emption for his neighbor. In al-Tajrid: If one makes his house a mosque, separates it, and makes its door to the road, then a house next to the mosque is sold — the waqf founder or trustee has no pre-emption due to lack of ownership. In al-Muhit and others: What is not permissible to sell among real estate, such as waqf and endowed shops, there is no pre-emption in that according to those who permit waqf. In al-Mabsut: If one buys land with small trees that then bear fruit, or with crops that then ripen, the pre-emptor may take that with all the fruit due to its attachment to the land.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by quoting the matn which defines what properties are not subject to shuf'a (pre-emption): a house sold without its land, a house given as dower, rent, khulu' consideration, blood money settlement, compensation for emancipation, a gift without compensation even if conditional, a sale with a stipulation of option for the seller, or a defective sale. The author then notes an inconsistency in the original author's definition of 'aqar (real estate) and clarifies that 'aqar includes land, estates (diyya'), and date palms. The commentary then provides further details from various sources (al-Muhit, al-Tatarkhaniyya, etc.) on what constitutes 'aqar for shuf'a purposes: bathhouses (only fixed parts), mills (only the lower stone), reeds rooted in the ground, but not fish. It also emphasizes that shuf'a only applies to owned land, not to lands granted by the Imam for cultivation, waqf properties, or property belonging to a mosque. The discussion follows the author's order, first clarifying the definition, then enumerating what is excluded.

Key Terms
عقار ('aqār): Immovable property including land, buildings, and trees rooted in the earth.
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to buy a property sold to a third party, compensating the buyer.
بلا عرصة (bilā 'arṣah): Without the land (i.e., the building or trees sold separately from the ground).
أجمة (ajamah): A thicket or marsh with reeds and possibly fish.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A house given as dower (mahr)
C) Land with trees bearing fruit
D) A bathhouse including its fixed parts

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a (pre-emption) is restricted to owned immovable property sold for monetary consideration; gifts, transfers without land, and property lacking private ownership do not trigger the right.

Shuf'a Does Not Apply to Movable Goods or Ships
Stating the Hanafi ruling on pre-emption for movables with proof and Maliki dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pre-emption for movable goods, explain the hadith evidence, and identify the difference between absolute and relative restriction.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is only for real estate, not for movable goods or ships, based on a hadith interpreted as a relative restriction.

Pre-emption (shuf'a) is a right granted to neighbors and co-owners to acquire property sold to a third party. But not all property is subject to this right. The Hanafi school, drawing on a key hadith, limits shuf'a to immovable property. In this lesson, we examine the ruling that shuf'a does not apply to movable goods (ʿaraḍ) or ships (fulk), and we see how the Hanafis defend this against the Maliki view that ships are akin to real estate.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify exactly what the matn says: 'لا تجب الشفعة في عرض وفلك' – shuf'a is not obligated in movable goods or ships.
The ruling is a clear negation of the right for these types of property.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'لا شفعة إلا في ربع أو حائط' – No shuf'a except in a house or garden. This hadith is used by the Hanafis to restrict shuf'a to immovables.
The hadith mentions only two specific types of immovables, but later you will see how this is interpreted.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection is that the hadith seems to restrict shuf'a absolutely to houses and gardens, which would exclude other immovables like vacant land. The opponent (Maliki school) points out that this would be an absurd result.
This objection forces the Hanafis to clarify the nature of the restriction.
4
Apply the Hanafi response: relative restriction
The Hanafis explain that the restriction is not absolute (qasr haqiqi) but relative (qasr idafi). The Prophet mentioned common examples, not an exhaustive list. Thus, shuf'a applies to all immovable property, but not to movables.
This interpretive move saves the hadith from absurdity and preserves the general principle that shuf'a is for immovables only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (لا في عرض وفلك) يعني لا تجب الشفعة في عرض وفلك . وقال مالك : تجب في السفينة لأنها تسكن كالعقار. ولنا ما روي عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قال «لا شفعة إلا في ربع أو حائط ولأن الأخذ بالشفعة ثبت على خلاف القياس فلا يجوز إلحاق المنقول به لأنه ليس في معنى العقار، وهذا الاستدلال فيه شيء فإن ظاهره حصر ثبوت الشفعة في الربح والحائط فدل ذلك على انتفاء حق الشفعة في غيرهما ومن غيرهما العروض والسفن، فيرد عليه أن مقتضى الحصر أن لا تثبت الشفعة في عقار غير ربح وحائط كضيعة خالية مثلاً وليس كذلك قطعاً فكيف يتمسك به؟ قلت يمكن حمل القصر على القصر الإضافي دون الحقيقي فالقصر بالنسبة إليهما لا بالنسبة إلى جميع ما عداهما فتأمل قال في العناية : الربع الدار والحائط البستان وأصله ما أحاط به .اهـ.
He (the author) said: '(Not in movable goods or ships)' meaning pre-emption is not obligated in movable goods or ships. Malik said: It is obligated in a ship because it is stationary like real estate. And our proof is what was narrated from him (peace be upon him) that he said: 'No pre-emption except in a house (rub') or a garden (ha'it).' And because the taking by pre-emption is established contrary to analogy, so it is not permissible to analogize movable property to it because it is not in the meaning of real estate. But there is something in this reasoning: its apparent meaning restricts the establishment of pre-emption to house and garden, so that indicates the negation of the right of pre-emption in other than these two, and among other things are movable goods and ships. But it is objected that the implication of the restriction is that pre-emption would not be established in real estate other than house and garden, such as a vacant estate for example, and that is certainly not the case, so how can one hold onto this? I say: it is possible to carry the restriction as relative, not absolute, so it is a restriction relative to these two, not to everything else. Contemplate this. He said in al-'Inayah: 'Rub' is the house and 'ha'it' is the garden, and its origin is what surrounds it.'
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that shuf'a does not apply to 'arad (movable goods) and fulk (ships). The author then reports Imam Malik's view that shuf'a applies to ships because they are stationary like real estate. The Hanafi proof is a hadith: 'No shuf'a except in a house (rub') or a garden (ha'it).' This hadith is taken to limit shuf'a to immovable property. The commentary then addresses an objection: the hadith seems to restrict shuf'a only to houses and gardens, which would exclude other immovables like vacant land. The author resolves this by explaining that the restriction is relative (qasr idafi), meaning the prophet was mentioning common examples, not an exhaustive list. Thus, shuf'a applies to all real estate, not just houses and gardens, but it does not extend to movables. The passage follows the order: state the ruling, present the opposing view, give the Hanafi proof, and answer a potential criticism.

Key Terms
عرض ('araḍ): Movable goods, as opposed to real estate.
فلك (fulk): Ship, vessel.
ربع (rub'): House, dwelling, specifically a residential building.
حائط (ḥā'iṭ): Garden, enclosed orchard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because it is stationary
B) No, because it is movable
C) Yes, if it is anchored
D) No, because the Prophet forbade it

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a is a right restricted to immovable property; movable goods and ships are not subject to pre-emption in Hanafi law.

Shuf'a in Buildings and Trees: Movable or Immovable?
Ruling that pre-emption does not apply to buildings or palm trees sold separately from land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether shuf'a applies to buildings and palm trees sold without land, and distinguish the case of an upper floor.

Core Idea
Buildings and trees are movable when sold without land — no shuf'a; but they follow the land if sold together.

What happens when someone sells a building but keeps the land? Or sells a palm tree separately? The Hanafi school distinguishes between movable and immovable property for the right of pre-emption (shuf'a). This lesson explains the basic rule and its exception for upper floors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for buildings and trees sold without land
Look at the matn: 'wa binā' wa nakhl bīʿā bilā ʿarṣah'. The author states that when a building or palm trees are sold without the land, shuf'a does not apply. This is because in Hanafi law, they are considered movable property.
Movable property (manqūl) is not subject to shuf'a.
2
Understand the rationale: they are movable
The text says 'li-annahumā manqūlān' (because they are movable). Shuf'a is a right that attaches to immovable property ('aqār). Buildings and trees, when detached from land ownership, lose their immovable character.
The underlying principle is the nature of shuf'a as a right in immovable property.
3
Apply the contrast: when sold with land
The matn implies the opposite: if the building or palm trees are sold together with the land, shuf'a becomes obligatory in the land and follows to the building/trees as a subsidiary (tabaʿan lahā).
The ruling changes based on whether the property is sold alone or with land.
4
Analyze the exception for upper floors
The text says 'bikhilāf al-'uluww' (unlike the upper floor). An upper floor is treated differently: it can be subject to shuf'a even without the land, because it is considered immovable. But only if it has a separate way from the lower floor.
If the upper floor shares a way with the lower floor, the way creates shared rights (khilṭa fī al-ḥuqūq).
5
Learn the further condition about shared way giving partner rights
The matn adds: if they share the same way, shuf'a can be claimed on the basis of being a partner in rights (khalīṭ fī al-ḥuqūq). This means even if the upper floor is sold separately, the neighbor may have shuf'a due to common access.
This is a special rule for upper floors; it does not apply to buildings or trees.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وبناء ونخل بيعا بلا عرصة) لأنهما منقولان فلا تجب فيهما إذا بيعا بلا أرض وإن بيعا معها تجب فيها الشفعة تبعاً لها بخلاف العلو حيث يستحق بالشفعة وتستحق به الشفعة على أنه مجاوره وذلك إذا لم يكن طريقه غير طريق السفل، وإن كان طريقهما واحداً يستحق بالطريق الشفعة على أنه خليط في الحقوق.
He (the author) said: 'And a building and palm trees sold without land' because they are movable, so pre-emption is not obligatory in them when sold without land. But if sold with the land, pre-emption is obligatory in them as a follow-up to it (the land). Unlike the upper floor (al-'uluww), where one acquires pre-emption through it and pre-emption is acquired due to it on the basis of being a neighbor, provided that its way is not different from the way of the lower floor. If they share the same way, one acquires pre-emption through the way on the basis of being a partner in rights.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn. The rule is clear: buildings and palm trees, when sold without the land (i.e., as separate chattels), are considered movable property and therefore not subject to shuf'a. However, if they are sold together with the land, shuf'a applies to the land and the buildings/trees follow as part of the immovable property. The passage then adds a nuance about upper floors ('uluww): if an upper floor is sold without the land, it is still subject to shuf'a because it is immovable? Actually the text says 'bikhilaf al-'uluww' meaning unlike the upper floor, which is treated differently: an upper floor can be the subject of shuf'a and can give rise to shuf'a for a neighbor, provided it has a separate way. If it shares a way with the lower floor, then the way creates a common right (khiltah fi al-huquq) that gives shuf'a. The commentary follows the order of the matn, explaining each part.

Key Terms
بناء (binā'): Building, structure.
نخل (nakhl): Palm trees.
عرصة ('arṣah): Land, courtyard, the ground upon which a building stands.
علو ('uluww): Upper floor or room.
خليط في الحقوق (khalīṭun fī al-ḥuqūq): Partner in rights (common access ways, utilities, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When sold alone, because they are valuable
B) When sold with the land
C) Never, because they are movable
D) Only if they are attached to the land

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a for buildings and trees depends on whether they are sold with land; without land they are movable and no shuf'a, but upper floors are an exception.

Shuf'a in Non-Monetary Transfers: Mahr, Khulu', and Blood Money
Ruling that pre-emption does not apply to a house acquired through non-monetary consideration like mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that shuf'a does not apply to a house given as mahr, khulu', or similar non-monetary consideration, and explain the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Shuf'a requires matching the buyer's exact consideration; non-monetary transfers have no equivalent to match.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses an important limitation on the right of pre-emption (shuf'a). The matn rules that when a house is acquired through non-monetary means such as dower (mahr), rent, khulu' compensation, or blood money settlement, the pre-emptor cannot claim shuf'a. We will explore the reason behind this ruling, the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute, and several detailed applications that clarify the boundaries of this right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
Note the cases listed where shuf'a is absent: house given as mahr, rent, khulu', blood money settlement, compensation for emancipation, gift without compensation. Understand the general rule: shuf'a only applies to monetary sales (mubādalat māl bi māl).
The key principle: the pre-emptor must take by the same cause as the buyer.
2
Examine the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that shuf'a applies to any transfer of property, and the pre-emptor pays market value when the exact consideration is unavailable.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different views of the scope of shuf'a.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi reply: shuf'a is an exception to general property rules, restricted to cases where the pre-emptor can match the exact cause (price). Non-monetary considerations cannot be matched, so shuf'a fails.
The response is based on the nature of shuf'a as a narrow right.
4
Understand intra-Hanafi disagreement
When the transaction includes both a non-monetary and a monetary component (e.g., house + cash as mahr), Imam Abu Hanifa says no shuf'a at all; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say shuf'a applies to the cash portion.
This shows the subtlety of applying the principle.
5
Apply to real cases
Work through the examples: marriage with dower only, sale for proper dower, peace (sulh) settlements, salam contracts, and khulu'. Determine whether shuf'a exists in each.
Practice distinguishing monetary vs. non-monetary exchanges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ودار جعلت مهراً أو أجرة أو بدل خلق أو بدل صلح عن دم عمد أو عوض عتق أو وهبت بلا عوض مشروط) لأن الشارع لم يشرع التملك بالشفعة إلا بما يملك به المشتري صورة ومعنى أو معنى بلا صورة ولا يمكن ذلك إذا تملك العقار بهذه الأشياء لأنها ليست بأموال ولا مثل لها حتى يأخذها الشفيع بمثلها فلم يمكن مراعاة شرط الشرع فيه التملك بما يملك به المشتري فلم يكن مشروعاً. وقال الإمام الشافعي: تجب فيهما وهو الشفعة فيأخذها بقيمتها عند تعذر الأخذ بمثلها بخلاف الهبة بلا عوض لتعذر الأخذ بلا عوض إذ هو غير مشروع ولنا ما تقدم ولأن الشفيع بتملك بما يملك به المشتري من السبب لا بسبب آخر، وههنا لو أخذه كان يأخذه بسبب آخر ولو تزوجها بغير مهر ثم فرض لها عقاراً مهراً لم يكن فيها الشفعة لأنه تعين بمهر المثل وهو مقابل بالبضع بخلاف ما لو باعها العقار بمهر المثل أو بالمسمى عند العقد أو بعده حيث تجب فيه الشفعة لأنه مبادلة مال بمال لأن ما أعطاه العقار بدل عما ذمته المهر. ولو تزوجها على دار على أن في من فلا شفعة في جميع الدار عند الإمام. وقالا تجب الشفعة في حصة الألف لأنه درهم مبادلة مال بمال في حقه ولهذا ينعقد بلفظ النكاح ولا يفسد بشرط النكاح وهو يقول معنى البيع فيه تابع فلا شفعة في الأصل فكذا في البيع ألا ترى أن المضارب إذا كان رأس ماله ألفاً ألفاً ثم اشترى بالألفين داراً في جوار رب المال ثم باعها بألفين فإن رب المال لا الشفعة في . حصة المضارب تبعاً لرأس المال لأن المضارب وكيل في حقه وليس في بيع الوكيل شفعة، وكذا في حق المضارب وهو البيع ؛ كذا في العتابية قوله «جعلت الدار مهراً» مثال . قال في العتابية : ولو قال صالحتك على أن تجعل هذه الدار مهراً لك وأعطيتك هذه الدار مهراً فلا شفعة للشفيع فيها. وقوله جعلت مهراً محترز عن البيع ولو باعها داراً بمهر مثلها أو صالحها على دار أو صالحها من دعوى حق على دار ففيهما الشفعة والقول قول المصالح في قيمة ذلك أو في قدره وفي السراجية : صالح في دار ادعاه على مائة درهم وهو جاحد لا شفعة فيها، فإن أقام الشفيع البينة أنها التي ادعاها فله الشفعة. وفي شرح الطحاوي : رجل تزوج امرأة ولم يسم مهراً ثم دفع لها داراً مهراً فهو على وجهين: إن قال الزوج جعلتها مهرك فلا شفعة فيها، وإن قال جعلتها بمهرك ألفاً ففيها الشفعة. وفي المحيط : لو خلع امرأته على ذلك على أن ترد عليه ألفاً فهو كما لو تزوج على دار على أن ترد عليه ألفاً كما تقدم. وفيه أيضاً أسلم داراً لرجل في مائة قفيز حنطة واستلم الدار فللشفيع أخذها بالشفعة، ولو افترقا قبل أن يقبض الدار بطل السلم ولا شفعة للشفيع اهـ. وفي العتابية : لا شفعة في دار هي بدل عن سكنى دار وخدمة عبد. وقيد بقوله «عن دم عمد» احترازاً . الخطأ . قال في المبسوط ولو كان عن جناية خطأ تجب الشفعة، لو صالح بها عن عن جنايتين أحدهما عمداً والأخرى خطأ فلا شفعة فيها على قول الإمام، وعندهما تجب فيها الشفعة فيما يخص جناية الخطأ، ولو صالح عن كفالة رجل بنفسه على دار فلا شفعة فيها لا لأن هذا صلح باطل .اهـ. قيد يقوله بلا عوض مشروط» لأنه لو شرط في العقد تجب الشفعة
He (the author) said: 'And a house made as dower, or rent, or consideration for khulu', or settlement for intentional killing, or compensation for emancipation, or given as a gift without conditional compensation) because the Lawgiver did not legislate ownership by pre-emption except by what the buyer owns in form and meaning, or in meaning without form, and that is not possible when the property is owned through these things because they are not wealth nor do they have an equivalent for the pre-emptor to take, so it is not possible to observe the condition of the Law of ownership by what the buyer owns, so it is not legislated. Imam al-Shafi'i said: Pre-emption is obligatory in these two, and the pre-emptor takes it by its value when taking by its equivalent is not possible, unlike a gift without compensation because taking without compensation is impossible since it is not legislated. And our proof is what preceded, and because the pre-emptor owns by the same cause that the buyer owns, not by another cause. Here if he took it, he would take it by another cause. If he married her without a dower then assigned a property as dower, there is no pre-emption in it because it is determined by the proper dower and is in exchange for the private part, unlike if he sold her the property for the proper dower or for the named dower at the contract or after, where pre-emption is obligatory because it is an exchange of property for property, because what he gave the property is in exchange for what is in his liability of dower. If he married her on condition of a house with a condition of one thousand [dirhams] then there is no pre-emption in the entire house according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Pre-emption is obligatory in the share of the thousand because it is an exchange of property for property in his right, and for this reason it is concluded with the word of marriage and is not invalidated by the condition of marriage. And he says the meaning of sale in it is subsidiary, so no pre-emption in the original, so likewise in the sale. Do you not see that if a mudarib has capital of one thousand, then he buys with two thousand a house adjacent to the owner of capital, then sells it for two thousand, the owner of capital has no pre-emption in the mudarib's share following the capital because the mudarib is an agent in his right, and there is no pre-emption in the sale of an agent. Similarly in the right of the mudarib himself as the seller. Thus in al-'Itabiyya. His statement 'made the house as dower' is an example. He said in al-'Itabiyya: If he says 'I have made peace with you that you make this house as your dower and I give you this house as dower,' then there is no pre-emption for the pre-emptor in it. His statement 'made as dower' is precautionary against sale. If he sold her a house for her proper dower, or made peace with her on a house, or made peace with her from a claim of right on a house, then there is pre-emption in both. And the statement of the one making peace is accepted regarding the value or amount. In al-Sirajiyya: He made peace on a house he claimed for one hundred dirhams while [the defendant] denies, there is no pre-emption in it. If the pre-emptor establishes evidence that it is the one he claimed, then he has pre-emption. In Sharh al-Tahawi: A man married a woman without naming a dower, then paid her a house as dower. There are two ways: if the husband says 'I made it your dower,' then no pre-emption in it; if he says 'I made it for your dower of one thousand,' then there is pre-emption in it. In al-Muhit: If he divorced her by khulu' on condition that she pay him one thousand, it is like if he married on a house on condition that she pay him one thousand, as previously. Also in it: If one pays in advance (salam) a house for one hundred qafiz of wheat and takes possession of the house, the pre-emptor may take it by pre-emption. If they separate before taking possession of the house, the salam is void and no pre-emption for the pre-emptor. In al-'Itabiyya: No pre-emption in a house that is compensation for dwelling in a house and the service of a slave. His restriction 'for intentional killing' is a precaution against [killing by] mistake. He said in al-Mabsut: If it was for a mistaken injury, pre-emption is obligatory. If he made peace for two injuries, one intentional and one mistaken, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa there is no pre-emption in it; according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) pre-emption is obligatory in what pertains to the mistaken injury. If he made peace for guaranteeing a man's person on a house, there is no pre-emption in it, not because this peace is void. His restriction 'without conditional compensation' is because if he stipulates compensation in the contract, pre-emption is obligatory.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute. The matn rules that when a house is given as mahr (dower), rent, khulu' compensation, blood money settlement, compensation for emancipation, or as a gift without conditional compensation, there is no shuf'a. The reason is that shuf'a is a right to take the property by paying the price that the buyer paid. Since these transactions do not involve a monetary price (or an equivalent that can be matched), the pre-emptor cannot exercise shuf'a. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees and holds that shuf'a applies, and the pre-emptor pays the market value. The Hanafi response reiterates that shuf'a requires the pre-emptor to take by the same cause as the buyer. The passage then provides numerous detailed cases: if a husband gives a house as mahr after marriage without specifying a dower, no shuf'a; but if he sells it for her dower, shuf'a applies. If the marriage contract includes a condition of a house plus a cash sum, Imam Abu Hanifa says no shuf'a in the whole house, while the Sahibayn say shuf'a in the cash portion. The analogy of a mudarib is used to illustrate agency. Further cases include salam contracts, khulu', and settlement for injuries. The commentary follows the author's order, explaining each exception and the differences of opinion within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): Dower, mandatory payment from husband to wife in marriage.
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by wife in exchange for compensation.
بدل صلح عن دم عمد (badal ṣulḥ 'an dam 'amd): Settlement for intentional killing, typically a monetary payment in lieu of retaliation.
عوض عتق ('iwaḍ 'itq): Compensation given for emancipation of a slave (e.g., kitabah).
مبادلة مال بمال (mubādalat māl bi māl): Exchange of property for property; a sale in its true sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does shuf'a apply to a house given as dower (mahr)?
A) Yes, because it is a transfer of property
B) No, because the pre-emptor cannot match the consideration
C) Yes, if the dower is equivalent to the value
D) No, because dower is not a valid transaction

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a only applies to monetary sales; non-monetary transfers like mahr, khulu', and blood money do not trigger shuf'a.

Shuf'ah in Non-Monetary Transfers: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on applying shuf'ah to gifts without compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school denies shuf'ah in gifts without compensation and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah requires taking by the same cause as the buyer; a gift has no price, so the shafi' cannot take without paying.

This lesson explores a fundamental dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the scope of shuf'ah (pre-emption). While shuf'ah is typically associated with sales, the question arises whether it applies to non-compensatory transfers such as gifts and charity. The Hanafi school insists on a strict condition: the shafi' must acquire the property by the same cause as the original buyer. Since a gift lacks compensation, the shafi' cannot replicate that cause, making shuf'ah inapplicable. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that shuf'ah should still apply with the shafi' paying market value. Understanding this disagreement clarifies the underlying principles of property rights in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding shuf'ah in gifts without compensation. Note the key condition: the shafi' must take by the same cause as the buyer.
The cause here is the means of acquisition (sale, gift, etc.).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Determine which school disagrees and what they hold. Imam al-Shafi'i allows shuf'ah even in gifts, with the shafi' paying the property's value when taking by equivalent is impossible.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i justification: they argue that shuf'ah is established to prevent harm, and that paying value is a sufficient substitute when matching the exact equivalent is not possible.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the shafi' would be taking by a different cause (paying money vs. receiving a gift), which violates the essential condition that ownership transfer must occur by the same means.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: under Hanafi law, a neighbor cannot claim shuf'ah on a gifted property; under Shafi'i law, he can, by paying market value.
This difference directly affects property transactions and neighbor rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاتجر وربح يستحق من يرد عليه به المشتري صورة ومعنى أو معنى بلا صورة ولا يمكن ذلك إذا تملك العقار بهذه الأشياء لأنها ليست بأموال ولا مثل لها حتى يأخذها الشفيع بمثلها فلم يمكن مراعاة شرط الشرع فيه التملك بما يملك به المشتري فلم يكن مشروعاً. وقال الإمام الشافعي: تجب فيهما وهو الشفعة فيأخذها بقيمتها عند تعذر الأخذ بمثلها بخلاف الهبة بلا عوض لتعذر الأخذ بلا عوض إذ هو غير مشروع ولنا ما تقدم ولأن الشفيع بتملك بما يملك به المشتري من السبب لا بسبب آخر، وههنا لو أخذه كان يأخذه بسبب آخر
So he traded and profited. The one who returns upon him [the buyer] deserves [the property] by what the buyer possessed, in form and meaning, or in meaning without form. That is not possible if he owned the property through these things because they are not wealth nor have equivalents so that the shafi' could take it by its equivalent, so the condition of the Law concerning ownership by what the buyer owned could not be observed, so it is not legislated. Imam al-Shafi'i said: Shuf'ah is obligatory in both [gift and sadaqah], so he takes it by its value when taking by its equivalent is impossible, unlike a gift without compensation because taking without compensation is impossible and is not legislated. Our evidence is what preceded, and because the shafi' owns by the same cause by which the buyer owns, not by another cause. Here, if he took it, he would be taking it by another cause.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the application of shuf'ah (pre-emption) to property acquired through non-monetary transfers such as gifts (hibah) and charity (sadaqah). The Hanafi position is that shuf'ah does not apply in such cases because the right of shuf'ah is established only when the shafi' can acquire the property by the same means as the original buyer. In a gift without compensation, the buyer acquired the property for free; the shafi' cannot take it without giving something, and taking without compensation is not legislated. Thus, the condition of matching the cause of acquisition fails. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that shuf'ah is still obligatory, and the shafi' should pay the market value when taking by equivalent is impossible. The Hanafi response reiterates the principle that the shafi' must own by the same cause, and here taking would be by a different cause. The discussion highlights a fundamental difference in the basis of shuf'ah between the schools.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): Pre-emption right: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property by paying its price, to prevent harm from a new owner.
هبة (hibah): Gift: a voluntary transfer of ownership without compensation.
مثل (mithl): Equivalent: a fungible good of the same kind and quality used for compensation in shuf'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does shuf'ah apply to a gift (hibah) given without any compensation?
A) Yes, shuf'ah applies and the shafi' pays the market value.
B) No, shuf'ah does not apply because the shafi' cannot take it without giving something in return.
C) Yes, but only if the donee has taken possession.
D) No, shuf'ah does not apply under any circumstances for gifts.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah in Hanafi fiqh requires the shafi' to take by the same cause as the buyer; thus, gifts without compensation are not subject to shuf'ah.

Shuf'ah in Marriage Dower: Imām vs Sahibayn
Intra-Ḥanafī dispute on shuf‘ah when a house is given as dower with a condition of payment back

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on shuf'ah in a marriage dower transaction and apply the tarjīḥ principle to determine the muftā bihi.

Core Idea
When marriage dominates, shuf'ah is excluded; only a pure property exchange triggers it.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced dispute within the Ḥanafī school concerning the right of pre-emption (shuf‘ah) when a house is given as marriage dower (mahr) with a condition that the wife pays back a sum to the husband. The point of contention is whether the transaction contains enough of a property exchange (mubādala māl bi māl) to warrant shuf‘ah. Understanding this ikhtilāf sharpens your ability to classify hybrid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hybrid transaction
Recognise a marriage where a house is dowered with a condition of payment from the wife to the husband. This blends nikāḥ (marriage) with a monetary transfer.
The payment is not the dower; it is an additional condition.
2
State Imām Abū Ḥanīfa's position
He holds no shuf‘ah in the entire house because the marriage element dominates; the sale aspect is subsidiary.
His proof: the mudārib analogy – an agent's sale carries no shuf‘ah because ownership is not absolute.
3
State the Ṣāḥibayn's position
Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad hold shuf‘ah in the portion corresponding to the payment (e.g., 1,000 dirhams), as that part is a genuine exchange of property for property.
They view the condition as creating a distinct commutative contract.
4
Analyse the underlying proofs
Imām: marriage is the primary contract; the payment is incidental. Ṣāḥibayn: the payment introduces an exchange that stands independently.
Both derive their views from the principle that shuf‘ah requires a pure sale (bay‘).
5
Determine the muftā bihī
The relied-upon position in the school is that of Imām Abū Ḥanīfa: no shuf‘ah. However, fatwā may consider the Ṣāḥibayn's view if the payment is explicitly structured as a separate sale.
Later Ḥanafī authorities (e.g., al-Marghīnānī) generally side with the Imām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو تزوجها بغير مهر ثم فرض لها عقاراً مهراً لم يكن فيها الشفعة لأنه تعين بمهر المثل وهو مقابل بالبضع بخلاف ما لو باعها العقار بمهر المثل أو بالمسمى عند العقد أو بعده حيث تجب فيه الشفعة لأنه مبادلة مال بمال لأن ما أعطاه العقار بدل عما ذمته المهر. ولو تزوجها على دار على أن في من فلا شفعة في جميع الدار عند الإمام. وقالا تجب الشفعة في حصة الألف لأنه درهم مبادلة مال بمال في حقه ولهذا ينعقد بلفظ النكاح ولا يفسد بشرط النكاح وهو يقول معنى البيع فيه تابع فلا شفعة في الأصل فكذا في البيع ألا ترى أن المضارب إذا كان رأس ماله ألفاً ألفاً ثم اشترى بالألفين داراً في جوار رب المال ثم باعها بألفين فإن رب المال لا الشفعة في . حصة المضارب تبعاً لرأس المال لأن المضارب وكيل في حقه وليس في بيع الوكيل شفعة، وكذا في حق المضارب وهو البيع ؛ كذا في العتابية قوله «جعلت الدار مهراً» مثال . قال في العتابية : ولو قال صالحتك على أن تجعل هذه الدار مهراً لك وأعطيتك هذه الدار مهراً فلا شفعة للشفيع فيها. وقوله جعلت مهراً محترز عن البيع ولو باعها داراً بمهر مثلها أو صالحها على دار أو صالحها من دعوى حق على دار ففيهما الشفعة والقول قول المصالح في قيمة ذلك أو في قدره وفي السراجية : صالح في دار ادعاه على مائة درهم وهو جاحد لا شفعة فيها، فإن أقام الشفيع البينة أنها التي ادعاها فله الشفعة. وفي شرح الطحاوي : رجل تزوج امرأة ولم يسم مهراً ثم دفع لها داراً مهراً فهو على وجهين: إن قال الزوج جعلتها مهرك فلا شفعة فيها، وإن قال جعلتها بمهرك ألفاً ففيها الشفعة. وفي المحيط : لو خلع امرأته على ذلك على أن ترد عليه ألفاً فهو كما لو تزوج على دار على أن ترد عليه ألفاً كما تقدم. وفيه أيضاً أسلم داراً لرجل في مائة قفيز حنطة واستلم الدار فللشفيع أخذها بالشفعة، ولو افترقا قبل أن يقبض الدار بطل السلم ولا شفعة للشفيع اهـ. وفي العتابية : لا شفعة في دار هي بدل عن سكنى دار وخدمة عبد. وقيد بقوله «عن دم عمد» احترازاً . الخطأ . قال في المبسوط ولو كان عن جناية خطأ تجب الشفعة، لو صالح بها عن عن جنايتين أحدهما عمداً والأخرى خطأ فلا شفعة فيها على قول الإمام، وعندهما تجب فيها الشفعة فيما يخص جناية الخطأ، ولو صالح عن كفالة رجل بنفسه على دار فلا شفعة فيها لا لأن هذا صلح باطل .اهـ. قيد يقوله بلا عوض مشروط» لأنه لو شرط في العقد تجب الشفعة
And if he marries her without a dower then later designates a property as dower, there is no shuf'ah in it because it is fixed as the equivalent dower and it is in exchange for the private part, unlike if he sold her the property for the equivalent dower or for the specified dower at the contract or after, where shuf'ah is obligatory because it is an exchange of property for property, since what he gave the property is a substitute for what was in his liability as dower. And if he marries her on a house with the condition that she [pay] a thousand [dirhams], there is no shuf'ah in the entire house according to Imam Abu Hanifa. They [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: shuf'ah is obligatory in the share of the thousand because it is a dirham exchange of property for property in his right, and therefore it is valid with the wording of marriage and is not invalidated by the condition of marriage, and he [Imam] says the meaning of sale in it is subsidiary, so no shuf'ah in the original, likewise in the sale. Do you not see that the mudarib if his capital was one thousand and then he bought with two thousand a house adjacent to the owner's property, then sold it for two thousand, the owner of capital has no shuf'ah in the mudarib's share because it is subsidiary to the capital, because the mudarib is an agent in his right and there is no shuf'ah in the sale of an agent, similarly in the right of the mudarib who is the seller; thus in Al-Atabiyya. His saying 'I made the house as dower' is an example. It is said in Al-Atabiyya: if he said 'I settled with you that you make this house as your dower and I give you this house as dower', there is no shuf'ah for the shafi' in it. And his saying 'I made it as dower' is a qualifier excluding sale. And if he sold her a house for its equivalent dower or settled with her on a house or settled with her from a claim of right on a house, there is shuf'ah in both. And the statement is the statement of the one making the settlement as to its value or its amount. And in Al-Sirajiyya: he settled in a house he claimed for one hundred dirhams while he [the defendant] denied, there is no shuf'ah in it; if the shafi' brings proof that it was the one he claimed, he has shuf'ah. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: a man married a woman without specifying a dower, then gave her a house as dower; it is on two faces: if the husband said 'I made it your dower', there is no shuf'ah in it; if he said 'I made it for your dower with a thousand [dirhams]', there is shuf'ah in it. And in Al-Muhit: if he divorced her by khul' on that condition that she returns a thousand to him, it is like if he married her on a house with the condition that she returns a thousand to him, as stated earlier. And also in it: he paid a house to a man for one hundred qafiz of wheat in salam and took possession of the house, the shafi' has the right to take it by shuf'ah; and if they separated before receiving the house, the salam is void and there is no shuf'ah for the shafi'. In Al-Atabiyya: there is no shuf'ah in a house that is a substitute for the residence of a house and the service of a slave. And the condition 'for intentional bloodshed' excludes accidental. It is said in Al-Mabsut: if the settlement is for accidental injury, shuf'ah is obligatory; if he settles by it for two injuries, one intentional and one accidental, there is no shuf'ah in it according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], and according to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] shuf'ah is obligatory in what pertains to the accidental injury. And if he settles for the guarantee of a man's person on a house, there is no shuf'ah in it, not because this settlement is void. The condition 'without a stipulated compensation' is a qualifier: because if he stipulates compensation in the contract, shuf'ah becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding shuf'ah in marriage contracts where a house is given as dower. The main scenario: a man marries a woman with a house as dower, but with a condition that she pays him back a sum (likely 1000 dirhams). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, no shuf'ah applies to the entire house because the transaction is predominantly a marriage, not a sale; the element of exchange (sale) is subsidiary. However, his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that shuf'ah applies to the portion corresponding to the payment (1000 dirhams) because that part is a genuine exchange of property. The discussion extends to various related cases: when a husband designates a house as dower after marriage without prior specification (no shuf'ah because it is in exchange for the marriage right), when he sells her a house for the dower (shuf'ah applies), and when he settles a claim against her by giving a house (shuf'ah applies). The text also covers compromises (sulh) for intentional vs. accidental killing: if the settlement is for intentional killing, no shuf'ah; if accidental, shuf'ah applies according to the companions. The passage illustrates the Hanafi principle that shuf'ah is only relevant when there is a genuine exchange of property for property (mubadala mal bi mal). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is generally that of Abu Hanifa, but there are scenarios where the companions' view is considered.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): Dower: the payment (usually property) given by the husband to the wife in marriage, which becomes her exclusive property.
صلح (sulh): Compromise: a settlement of a dispute by mutual agreement, often involving a transfer of property.
عوض (iwad): Compensation: something given in exchange for property or a right; can be fungible or non-fungible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imām Abū Ḥanīfa, if a man marries a woman and gives her a house as dower with the condition that she pays him back 1000 dirhams, what is the ruling on shuf'ah?
A) Shuf'ah applies to the entire house.
B) Shuf'ah applies only to the part corresponding to the 1000 dirhams.
C) No shuf'ah applies to the entire house.
D) Shuf'ah applies only after the 1000 dirhams are paid.

Key Takeaway
In the Ḥanafī school, when marriage and property transfer are combined, the marriage dominates, precluding shuf'ah; only a clear separation of exchange triggers the right.
